كالشارح لما هنالك
ثالثاً - جاء ( المعيار » خصباً بالأمثلة ، بينما نرى « المِحَك »
يقتصر فيه على الحاجي منها ، ولكنه موف بالغرض وزيادة . رابعاً - يضاف إلى هذا إثارة بعض المسائل في « المِحَك » لا نجدها في ( المعيار ) كما أنه أثار مثلها فيه مما لا نجدها في ( المِحَك ) ، ولهذا يقول أهل العلم : ( ما أغنى كتاب عن كتاب ) . أما بشأن الترتيب والتحقيق في المحك» وهما في غاية البلج فيه .. فليسا وقفاً عليه ، بل هي سمة سارية في مؤلفاته أرضاه مولاه ؛ فقد بيّن في مطلع ( المستصفى » أن الطالب بحاجة للترتيب لأجل الحفظ ، وبحاجة للتحقيق لأجل الفهم ، وهذا سر من أسرار ذيوع المكتبة الغزالية .
Difference between 'al-Mi'yar' and 'al-Mihakk'
Among the names of al-Mihakk that have circulated in library circles: 'The Compendium of Understanding in the Origin of Sciences' and 'Introduction to the Sciences' — two names that suggest the generalization of al-Mihakk as a reference book in the art of logic. But what are the most prominent differences between it and al-Mi'yar?
These differences can be summarized as follows:
First — al-Mihakk did not concern itself with the logical terminology circulating in philosophical books, since the intent was not to engage philosophers in debate, but to implant this science — whose inquiries have been difficult for many students — far from the whirlpool of terminology, and to prevent them from clinging to the form at the expense of the meaning. This is what the Imam did not adhere to in al-Mi'yar; therein he adopted the language of the people (philosophers) and engaged them with it, and even defined their terminologies that were like secret messages sent, hinting at changing the language of debate in his time, as it had become widespread among them that the jurists did not know their terminology nor grasp their purposes. Hence the arrows of 'al-Tahafut', 'al-Maqasid', and 'al-Mi'yar' were lying in wait for them.
Second — conciseness and richness of material together in al-Mihakk, whereas in al-Mi'yar we find the Imam's pen roams and expands the discussion until he sees he has thoroughly covered the topic. Both have their importance for the student of knowledge; for al-Mihakk is like a quick catch that one does not leave under any circumstances, and al-Mi'yar is like an expounder of what is there.
Third — al-Mi'yar came rich in examples, while we see al-Mihakk limits itself to the necessary ones, but it is sufficient for the purpose and more.
Fourth — added to this is the raising of some issues in al-Mihakk that we do not find in al-Mi'yar, just as it raised similar issues in al-Mi'yar that we do not find in al-Mihakk. For this reason the people of knowledge say: 'How self-sufficient is one book from another.' As for arrangement and verification in al-Mihakk — they are at the highest degree of clarity in it — they are not exclusive to it, but rather a pervasive feature in his works, may Allah be pleased with him. For he stated at the beginning of al-Mustasfa that the student needs arrangement for memorization and needs verification for understanding. This is one of the secrets of the widespread distribution of al-Ghazali's library.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a comparative introduction highlighting the key differences between two of al-Ghazali's works on logic: al-Mihakk and al-Mi'yar. The author lists four differences. The first difference is that al-Mihakk avoids the technical terminology of the philosophers, aiming to make logic accessible to students who struggle with complex terms, whereas al-Mi'yar fully engages with the philosophers' language. The second difference is that al-Mihakk is concise yet rich, while al-Mi'yar is expansive and detailed. Each has its benefit: al-Mihakk is like a quick catch that one never abandons, al-Mi'yar like a commentator explaining thoroughly. The third difference is that al-Mi'yar is abundant in examples, while al-Mihakk uses only necessary ones but still suffices. The fourth difference is that each book contains unique issues not found in the other, illustrating the principle that 'one book is self-sufficient from another.' Finally, the author remarks that both arrangement and verification are hallmarks of al-Ghazali's works, as he stated in al-Mustasfa that arrangement aids memorization and verification aids understanding.

Key Terms
المعيار
المحاك
الترتيب
التحقيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) al-Mi'yar uses only necessary examples, while al-Mihakk uses abundant examples.
B) al-Mihakk uses abundant examples, while al-Mi'yar uses only necessary ones.
C) al-Mi'yar is rich in examples, while al-Mihakk limits itself to necessary ones.
D) Both use the exact same examples.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali tailored his logical works to different needs: al-Mi'yar for comprehensive engagement with philosophical discourse, al-Mihakk for accessible foundational understanding.

The Pedagogical Value of Arrangement (Tartib) and Verification (Tahqiq)
Author highlights al-Ghazali's emphasis on arrangement and verification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how al-Ghazali's principles of arrangement (for memorization) and verification (for understanding) apply to learning logic.

Core Idea
Memorization through order, understanding through depth: al-Ghazali's twin keys to mastering Islamic sciences.

The passage concludes by highlighting that arrangement and verification are hallmarks of al-Ghazali's works, especially in logic. Here we unpack these two pedagogical principles and see how they enhance learning.

Guided Path
1
Define arrangement (tartib)
Find where al-Ghazali states that the student needs arrangement for memorization. What does arrangement mean here?
Think about logical ordering of topics.
2
Define verification (tahqiq)
Identify the other need: verification for understanding. How does verification differ from simple memorization?
Consider critical engagement with material.
3
See the combination
Notice that arrangement alone is insufficient; verification is also needed. How do they complement each other?
This is a recurring theme in Islamic education.
4
Apply to al-Ghazali's works
How do al-Mi'yar and al-Mihakk exemplify these principles? Consider their structure and depth.
Which work emphasizes which principle more?
5
Apply to your own study
Identify one subject you are studying and plan how to use tartib and tahqiq to master it.
Be specific: list topics in order and a verification method.

The Unconventional Order of al-Mihakk: Syllogism Before Definition
Author explains that al-Mihakk places syllogism first, unlike later logicians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the difference in topical ordering between al-Mihakk and later logic texts and explain the pedagogical significance of placing syllogism first.

Core Idea
Al-Mihakk flips the standard order: syllogism first, definition later.

This passage alerts you to a distinctive feature of al-Mihakk: it begins with syllogism (qiyas) rather than definition (hadd). If you are used to later works like al-Sanusiyya or al-Isharat, this reversal may seem strange. The author calls you to set aside the familiar and embrace the beneficial arrangement of this primary reference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing orders
Locate in the Arabic where the author describes the order in al-Mihakk (القياس صدر الكتاب) and contrasts it with the order in later works (مؤخَّراً عن بحث الحدّ). Note that al-Mihakk starts with syllogism, while later texts start with definition.
The key Arabic phrase: 'تطالعنا أبحاث القياس صدر الكتاب، وهو يُلفى عند المتأخرين مؤخَّراً عن بحث الحدّ للتصورات'.
2
Understand the author's admonition
Read the sentence starting with 'وعلى المرء الفطام المألوف...' The author advises you to wean yourself from the familiar, pay attention to what is beneficial (النافع), and train to adopt al-Mihakk's method. Reflect on why an author might deliberately change the order.
3
Relate to the broader structure of logic texts
The passage hints that the usual poles (أقطاب) of logic include the division into concepts (تصورات) and assents (تصديقات). Note that al-Mihakk's departure from the typical sequence is intentional, not a mistake.
This step prepares you to analyze the pedagogical rationale behind different arrangements.

Al-Ghazali's Method of Terminology
Al-Ghazali explains his rationale for inventing a unique logical terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Ghazali's rationale for inventing a unified logical terminology and identify the three terminological traditions he mentions.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali invented terminology to unify logic across theological, legal, and philosophical traditions.

In this passage, al-Ghazali explains why he created new logical terms rather than simply following one existing tradition. He identifies three distinct terminological sets—those of the theologians, jurists, and logicians—and warns that sticking to only one would limit the student's understanding. Instead, he crafted a hybrid vocabulary, using common words where possible and inventing new ones where needed, so that learners could later navigate any text in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terminological traditions
Find in the Arabic the three groups al-Ghazali mentions: al-mutakallimun (theologians), al-fuqaha' (jurists), and al-mantiqiyyun (logicians). These are the three traditions whose technical terms he seeks to bridge.
Notice he lists them explicitly: اصطلاح المتكلمين، والفقهاء، والمنطقيين.
2
Understand the problem with following only one tradition
Al-Ghazali says that if he had followed only one group, the student's understanding would be limited to that group's terminology, leaving them unable to comprehend the other two.
He uses the phrase 'يقصر فهمك عليه' (your understanding would be confined to it).
3
See his solution: common words and new inventions
He chose words that were common among all three groups, and invented new words that none of them used. This creates a neutral, pedagogical vocabulary.
The terms he invented are those they did not share in using: 'واخترعت ألفاظاً لم يشتركوا في استعمالها'.
4
Grasp the pedagogical goal
The ultimate aim is that once the student learns the meanings through these unified terms, they can then understand any book in the field, because they will be able to map unfamiliar terms back to the core meanings.
He says: 'فإذا فهمت المعاني بهذه الألفاظ... فما تصادفه في سائر الكتب يمكنك أن تردَّه إليها'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال رحمه الله تعالى في هذه الاصطلاحات المخترعة : ( إني اخترعت أكثرها من تلقاء نفسي ؛ لأن الاصطلاحات في هذا الفن ثلاثة ؛ اصطلاح المتكلمين ، والفقهاء ، والمنطقيين ، ولم أوثر أن أتبع واحداً منهم فيقصر فهمك عليه ، ولا تفهم اصطلاح الفريقين الآخرين ، ولكن استعملت من الألفاظ ما رأيته كالمتداول بين جميعهم ، واخترعت ألفاظاً لم يشتركوا في استعمالها ، حتى إذا فهمت المعاني بهذه الألفاظ .. فما تصادفه في سائر الكتب يمكنك أن تردَّه إليها ، وتطلع على مرادهم منها ) (١) . (۱) انظر ( ص ۱۰۲ ) .
And he (may God have mercy on him) said regarding these invented terminologies: 'I invented most of them from my own mind, because the terminologies in this art are three: the terminology of the theologians, the jurists, and the logicians. I did not prefer to follow one of them so that your understanding would be limited to it, and you would not understand the terminology of the other two groups. Rather, I used words that I saw as common among all of them, and I invented words that they did not share in using, so that when you understand the meanings by these words... then whatever you encounter in other books you can refer back to them, and you will become aware of what they intend by them.' (1). (1) See p. 102.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a methodological justification by al-Ghazali for his terminological choices. He states that he invented most of his terms himself. The reason is that there are three distinct terminological traditions in logic: that of the theologians (mutakallimun), the jurists (fuqaha'), and the logicians (mantiqiyyun). If he had followed only one, the student would only understand that tradition and be confused by the others. Therefore, he selected words that are common across all three, and invented new words that none of them used. The goal is pedagogical: once the student learns the meanings through these unified terms, they can then understand any book because they will be able to map the terms back to these core meanings. This shows al-Ghazali's concern for practical learning and his desire to bridge different scholarly traditions.

Key Terms
الاصطلاحات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did al-Ghazali not follow the terminology of only one group?
A) He disliked all three traditions.
B) He wanted to restrict understanding to one tradition.
C) He wanted the student to understand all three traditions.
D) He was forced by his teachers.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's terminological innovation was driven by pedagogy: to create a universal vocabulary that opens access to all three major logical traditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Reading Manuscript Descriptions in a Critical Edition
Lists the manuscripts and printed edition used to produce the critical text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of a manuscript description in a critical edition of an Islamic logical work.

Core Idea
Manuscript descriptions are the chain of custody for the text's authenticity.

When you open a modern critical edition of an Islamic text, the front matter includes descriptions of the manuscripts used. This is not mere formality—it tells you the age, quality, and lineage of each source. In this lesson, you will learn to parse such a description and understand what each element means for the reliability of the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the copy number and siglum
Each manuscript is given a letter (أ, ب, etc.). Find that first. The siglum is used to refer to the manuscript in the critical apparatus.
Look for 'رمز لها بـ' followed by a letter.
2
Locate the repository and shelf number
Find where the manuscript is held (library/collection) and its call number. This verifies the manuscript actually exists and can be consulted.
E.g., 'دار الكتب المصرية تحت رقم (143) منطق تيمور'.
3
Note the physical description and contents
Observe the binding, number of pages/folios, and what other works are bound with it. This helps identify the manuscript and its completeness.
E.g., 'مجلدة لطيفة جمعت بين المحك وفيصل التفرقة'.
4
Check the date and scribe information
Look for the copying date (year) and the name of the scribe. This indicates the age of the manuscript and potential for errors.
E.g., 'تم نسخ كتابنا سنة (1109 هـ) على يد كاتبه عبد الله بن رمضان'.
5
Evaluate any critical remarks
Note if the editor comments on the manuscript's quality or suspicious features. This affects how much trust to place in that copy.
E.g., 'جاء تاريخ كتابتها (445 هـ) وهو خطأ ظاهر'.

وصف النسخ الخطية
اعتمد في إخراج هذا الكتاب سبع نسخ؛ ست خطيَّةٌ وواحدة مطبوعة متقدمة، قد تظافرت بمجموعها في إخراجه، وهي: النسخة الأولى: محفوظة بدار الكتب المصرية بالقاهرة، تحت رقم (١٤٣) منطق تيمور؛ وهي مجلدة لطيفة جمعت بين المحك وفيصل التفرقة للإمام الغزالي أيضاً، وقع المِحَك من أولها إلى الصفحة (١٤٥)، كتبت بخط معتاد، ولم يقع فيها تاريخ. ورمز لها بـ (أ).
النسخة الثانية: محفوظة بدار الكتب المصرية بالقاهرة، تحت رقم (٩٦٧ مجاميع طلعت)؛ وهي مجموع وقع المِحَك فيه من الورقة (۳۸) إلى الورقة (۸۱)، كتب بخط نسخي معتاد، وتم نسخ كتابنا سنة (۱۱۰۹ هـ) على يد كاتبه عبد الله بن رمضان بن إسماعيل. ورمز لها بـ (ب).
النسخة الثالثة: محفوظة بدار الكتب المصرية بالقاهرة أيضاً، تحت رقم خاص (٧٦٠٤)، (۲۲۷) مجاميع طلعت)؛ وهي مجموع ضمَّ جملة من كتب المصنف: القسطاس المستقيم وفيصل التفرقة، وإلجام العوام، والمضنون به على غير أهله، ومِحَك النظر، ومعه رسالة لشيخه إمام الحرمين، وهي مغيث الحق كما جاء فيها. وقد وقع المِحَك فيه من الورقة (٩٥) إلى الورقة ۱۳۱)، وكتب آخرها بخط مستعجل، وكان تمام نسخها سنة (١٠٠٧ هـ). ورمز لها بـ (ج).

Description of the manuscripts. Seven copies were relied upon in producing this book: six manuscripts and one early printed edition, which together cooperated in its production. They are: The first copy: Preserved in the Egyptian National Library in Cairo, under number (143) Mantiq Taymur; it is a nice bound volume that brings together al-Mihakk and Faysal al-Tafriqa by Imam al-Ghazali as well; al-Mihakk occurs from its beginning to page (145); written in a regular hand; it bears no date. The fourth copy: A copy from the Academy of Sciences of Tashkent, photographed at Jum'a al-Majid Center, under number (3907); it is a part of a collection; al-Mihakk occurs in it from leaf (108) to leaf (164); its date of copying is given as (445 AH) which is clearly an error; perhaps it dates back to the sixth century AH. It is a good copy, in which many scribal errors found in other copies have been corrected. It is designated by (D).

Each description includes the library location, shelf number, contents, physical features, and any notable details such as scribe name or date. The text notes that manuscript D has a suspicious date (445 AH) and suggests it is actually from the sixth century AH. This information helps the reader understand the textual basis of the edition.

How Manuscript Editions Preserve Logical Works
Describes manuscripts and editorial method

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of an editor's methodology for publishing a classical logical text.

Core Idea
Textual criticism is the foundation for accurate study of classical Islamic logic.

Before you can analyse the logical arguments in a text like al-Ghazali's al-Mihakk, you must have a reliable edition. This lesson walks you through the editorial process described in the introduction, showing how editors collate manuscripts, record variants, and produce a usable text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the manuscripts used
Read the descriptions of the sixth and seventh copies. Note their origin, physical condition, and any distinguishing features. The editor gives each a symbol (و for the sixth, ط for the seventh).
Manuscript symbols are crucial for cross-referencing footnotes.
2
Understand the editor's treatment of each copy
The sixth copy is from the Institute of Arabic Manuscripts, photographed from a Sana'a manuscript. It is described as 'good', collated and corrected. The seventh is a printed edition with some typographical errors. The editor uses both, but relies more on the manuscript.
Editors often assign varying weights to witnesses based on their quality.
3
Learn the editorial methodology steps
The editor lists seven steps: collation, full vocalisation, punctuation, citing Qur'anic verses in Uthmani script, citing hadiths, adding side headings, and providing a brief biography. These are standard for serious editions.
Each step serves to make the text accessible and accurate for scholars.
4
Apply this knowledge to a logical text
Consider how this methodology ensures that logical arguments are transmitted without corruption. Poor editorial work can introduce errors that misrepresent the author's reasoning.
Always check the editor's methodology before citing a classical text.

The sixth copy: a copy from the Institute of Arabic Manuscripts in Kuwait, photographed from a copy preserved in the House of Manuscripts in Sana'a; it is a collection containing the book "al-Mihakk". It is a good copy, written in a common script, spanning from folio 19 to folio 74. The copying was completed in the year 907 AH, and it is collated and corrected as indicated at its end. We designate it with (W).
The seventh copy: printed by the Literary Press in Egypt, under the care of the two eminent scholars: Muhammad Badr al-Din al-Na'sani al-Halabi and Mustafa al-Qabbani al-Dimashqi. It comprises 134 pages, and the effort expended in its production is evident, but it did not avoid a number of typographical errors, and it was utilized in several places. We designate it with (T).
32
Methodology of the Work in the Book
The relied-upon manuscript copies, including the printed edition of Allama Badr al-Din al-Na'sani al-Halabi and Mustafa al-Qabbani al-Dimashqi, have cooperated to produce "al-Mihakk" in the best possible manner. The stages of attention proceeded as follows:
- Copying the book and collating it with its relied-upon manuscript sources, recording the most important differences and additions occurring in some copies.
- Fully vocalizing the book with complete grammatical vowel marks, and fully dotting what might be ambiguous.
- Adorning the book with punctuation marks according to the scientific method followed in the center.
- Citing the Qur'anic verses and recording them in the 'Uthmani script according to the recitation of Hafs from 'Asim, may Allah have mercy on them.
- Citing the hadiths and traditions from their primary sources with reference to their narrators and sources.
- Placing side headings to help the reader understand the sections of the book.
- Providing a brief biography of the author, Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali, appropriate to the book at hand.

This passage is from the editor's introduction to an edition of al-Ghazali's "al-Mihakk". It describes two of the manuscript copies used: the sixth copy (from the Institute of Arabic Manuscripts, designated 'W') and the seventh copy (the printed edition, designated 'T'), along with their physical characteristics and provenance. Then it outlines the editorial methodology: collation of manuscripts, full vocalization, punctuation, quotation of Qur'anic verses in the 'Uthmani script, citation of hadiths, addition of side headings, and a brief biography of the author. This is standard editorial front matter, not a logical argument.

Understanding the Editorial Methodology of a Critical Edition
Describes the editorial methodology for the critical edition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key steps in the editorial methodology used for Mahakk al-Nazar and evaluate their importance for studying Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
A critical edition is a scholarly reconstruction, not a simple republication.

Before studying al-Ghazali's Mahakk al-Nazar, it is essential to understand how the text reached you. This passage describes the meticulous editorial process: collating manuscripts, vocalizing, referencing sources. These steps ensure the text is reliable for logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the editorial goals
The passage states the aim is to produce the text 'in the most desirable manner'. That means relying on reliable manuscript copies and carefully verifying the text. This is the foundation for any critical edition.
The goal is not simply to reproduce a text, but to reconstruct an authoritative version.
2
List the sequential steps
The passage enumerates nine stages. Start by listing them: copying/collating manuscripts, full vocalization, punctuation, Quranic reference, hadith reference, side headings, biography, study of the book, and explaining ambiguous words.
Each step serves a distinct purpose; note that some are about text accuracy, others about reader guidance.
3
Distinguish substantive from procedural steps
Substantive steps directly affect the text's accuracy (collation, vocalization). Procedural steps aid the reader (side headings, biography). Both are important but for a logic text, accuracy is paramount for analyzing arguments.
For example, full vocalization prevents misreading logical terms; punctuation clarifies proposition boundaries.
4
Evaluate the importance for logical study
Why does footnoting sources matter for studying a logic text? Because al-Ghazali uses Quranic verses and hadith as examples in logical arguments. Accurate referencing allows you to verify the logical examples and understand their context.
Without this, you might misinterpret a logical argument that relies on a specific verse's meaning.

Methodology of work on the book. The reliable manuscript copies cooperated, including the printed edition of Badr al-Din al-Na'sani al-Halabi and Mustafa al-Qabbani al-Dimashqi, to produce 'al-Mihak' in the most desirable manner. The stages of care proceeded as follows: - Copying the book and collating it against its reliable manuscript origins, recording the most important differences and additions found in some copies. - Fully vocalizing the book with complete grammatical diacritics, including marking what might be ambiguous. - Adorning the book with punctuation marks according to the scientific methodology followed in the center. - Referencing Quranic verses, presenting them in the Uthmani script according to the recitation of Hafs from Asim, may Allah have mercy on them both. - Referencing hadiths and traditions from their primary sources, indicating their narrators and sources. - Placing side headings to assist the reader in understanding the sections of the book. - A brief biography of the author, Huijat al-Islam al-Ghazali, appropriate to the book at hand. - Preparing a brief study of the book and the author's objective. - Explaining some words that might be ambiguous, though this is very rare. In conclusion: We ask Allah to accept this effort and grant more assistance and care. The Scientific Committee at Dar al-Minhaj Center for Studies and Scientific Verification, Wednesday, 5 Rabi' al-Awwal 1437 AH, corresponding to December 16, 2015 CE.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is the editors' description of the methodology used in preparing the critical edition of Mahakk al-Nazar. It opens by stating that reliable manuscript copies were used, including printed editions, to produce the text. Then it lists the stages of editorial work: first, copying and collating manuscripts with recording variants; second, fully vocalizing the text with diacritical marks; third, adding punctuation; fourth, verifying and referencing Quranic verses in the Uthmani script; fifth, verifying and referencing hadiths from their primary sources; sixth, adding side headings to help readers; seventh, including a brief biography of al-Ghazali; eighth, preparing a study of the book and its objectives; and ninth, explaining some ambiguous terms, though this is rare. The passage concludes with a supplication and the date and committee signature. This is editorial front matter, not part of Ghazali's original text.

Reading a Critical Edition: The Editorial Note
Editorial note describing the book's preparation and the scientific committee.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of an editorial note and evaluate its purpose in a critical edition.

Core Idea
Front matter is not filler — it tells you who vetted the text and what to expect.

When you open a critical edition of a classical text, the editorial note may seem like a formality. But it contains crucial information: who is responsible for the edition, what methodology was used, and what limitations exist. This lesson teaches you to read such notes as a scholar would — with attention to the authority behind the edition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the publisher and committee
Look for who produced the edition. Here it is the Scientific Committee at Dar al-Minhaj Center. Their names may not be given, but their institutional affiliation suggests a certain level of rigor.
2
Note what the edition includes
The note says it includes a brief study of the book and the author's objective, and explanation of some problematic terms (rare). This tells you what added value the edition provides.
3
Look for the date and manuscript sources
The date and mention of images of relied-upon manuscripts. This is important for knowing which manuscript base was used.
4
Evaluate the limitations
The note says explanations of problematic terms are 'very rare'. This indicates that the edition does not provide extensive commentary on difficult passages. You should be aware of what the edition does not do.
5
Assess credibility
The committee's name and the date suggest a modern scholarly edition. Check if the committee is known for rigorous verification. This affects how much you can trust the text.

Al-Ghazali's Call: Escape Taqlid, Seek Insight
Author begins with praise, prayer, and a request for the reader's supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Ghazali's warning against blind imitation (taqlid) and explain why he calls for insight over imitation in seeking truth.

Core Idea
Truth is attained through insight, not by blindly following others.

This opening passage from Mahakk al-Nazar is not merely a pious introduction; it sets the epistemological foundation for the entire work. Al-Ghazali addresses the reader with a request for supplication and a clear warning: do not fall into the 'morass of imitation' (حضيض التقليد). Instead, he urges the reader to ride the path of danger – the difficult road of independent critical thinking. Understanding this stance is crucial before engaging with the logical tools he will present.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening praise
Notice that al-Ghazali acknowledges human limitation in praising Allah. This humility signals that knowledge is a divine gift, not a human possession.
2
Identify the addressee
Find the phrase 'son of prohibition in religion' (ابن المنع في الدين). It likely refers to a student who refrains from blind following, seeking truth.
Interpretation based on context; OCR corruption obscures the exact phrase.
3
Locate the warning against taqlid
Search for the phrase 'rising above the morass of imitation' (الارتفاع على حضيض التقليد). This is the key epistemological stance: truth requires effort, not imitation.
The word حضيض means low ground, suggesting taqlid is a base state.
4
Understand the alternative
Al-Ghazali proposes 'striving for insight' (الاطلاع) and sincerity. He promises that even if one fails to reach the truth, the effort is rewarded; if one deviates, the effort still illuminates.
This reflects a merit-based view of intellectual struggle.
5
Connect to the book's purpose
The passage frames Mahakk al-Nazar as a tool for independent verification, not a collection of doctrines. The reader is invited to engage critically.
This sets the tone for the logical lessons that follow.

In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah, praise that despite its abundance does not exhaust His right to what is lawful, and I thank Him outwardly even if through its extension it brings forth my faults, and all is from His action. He does not require of Him from praise and gratitude except according to one's capacity and benefit. And may Allah send blessings upon Muhammad, His servant and messenger, the best of His creation and His friends. To proceed: the truthfulness of your judgment, O son of prohibition in religion, may Allah gather us and you among the bearers of the standards, and in it is the editing of the touchstone of consideration and the safeguard, and may He protect you from the places of error in the narrow passages of reflection. Indeed, from the Arab constriction and it sent in my soul ... and around me to an art I discarded by the rule of the poison and the amputation, I lost to it a departure from one who looks at what he finds and disregard for looking at one who accepts and the narcissism of Allah. From the hopes I ask from the blessing of your supplication at the time of your departure, and that in your seclusions from us, rights in what you yourself have trusted, except that it is accepted by your pleasure, it was explained to you? ... that you not remove [yourself] from supplication, and that you share from your separation points between the Adamites, a barrier of righteousness. A supplication for the believer alone and his followers. What do you do? So we rely on individuals and the script, and the provision of the night is meager, and the presence of criticism is counterfeit, and the praised is abundant, and the Lord is powerful, and virtue is worthy of saying: 'Our defects are a destruction of a Muslim, one.' Because of that, may Allah pray with difficulty. And here I am proposing to you that you say: 'May Allah grant you that the truth is truth and provide you with its following, and falsehood is falsehood and provide you with its avoidance.' Whoever rides the path of danger in rising above the morass of imitation, with safety of meaning to the regions of insight, early with the danger of his energy and the knowledge of his family, for if he does not attain the truth, all his effort is a gift; and if he deviates from his origin, all his effort is a beacon; and his obedience with the rebels is a secret, so we return ... his play and from the air with the general, what your ambition heard and your nobility fell. The symbol of the first leaf of copy (A) and the increase of alteration, and the measures have fallen short of guiding it. If the term ends and is pushed by obstacles and my victory was not ... and it is cut off on its building by refining what must be attracted from it, you encounter in it what I entrust to you in this book. As for now, Muhammad and my service to you by persisting in supplication, asking Allah Almighty to rectify our building in what He has given from authoring a book and benefiting knowledge with no intention except purely the face of Allah, good mixed with a state of its prevention from this world. And what a good state of one who abandons creation in the path of bravery while he turns to this world, and leaves the created to it while he turns away from it. We seek refuge with Allah from the evils of ourselves and our bad deeds, and we ask Allah Almighty to rectify our conditions, our words, and our actions, for He is the God of answering the prayer and the flowing of His generosity. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds. The book on distinguishing between and heresy, authored by Imam, the mountain of Islam, the praised, al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him. Commentary
This passage is the opening front matter of the book 'Mahakk al-Nazar' by Imam al-Ghazali. It begins with the basmalah and a flowery praise of Allah, acknowledging that all praise and gratitude are within human capacity. The author then sends blessings on the Prophet Muhammad. He addresses a reader or student (possibly 'son of prohibition in religion'), asking for mutual supplication and protection from error in logical reasoning. The text reflects al-Ghazali's humility and his request for the reader's prayers. He warns against blind imitation (taqlid) and encourages striving for insight with sincerity. The passage ends with the title of the book and a note about manuscript copies. Due to heavy OCR corruption, the exact phrasing is uncertain in many places, but the overall tone is that of a traditional scholarly invocation and introduction.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Opening Invocation and Purpose of the Book
Author opens with praise and sets the book's purpose as a logical safeguard.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the chief purpose of al-Ghazali's Mahakk al-Nazar as a critical tool for safeguarding reasoning from error.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's touchstone is a logical scale to protect from error in speculation.

This opening passage from Mahakk al-Nazar begins with praise of God and blessings on the Prophet, typical of classical Islamic works. But it quickly announces the book's central mission: to provide a 'touchstone of speculation' (miḥakk al-naẓar) that serves as a scale ('mi'yār) for distinguishing truth from falsehood, especially in difficult points of inference. The author prays to be among the 'bearers of the scales' and to be protected from error in the 'straits of consideration.' This lesson unpacks those metaphors to understand the epistemological purpose of the work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key metaphors
Find the terms 'touchstone' (miḥakk), 'scales' (maʿāyīr), and 'straits of consideration' (maḍāʾiq al-iʿtibār). These are not just poetic images; they indicate that the book provides a criterion for testing reasoning, a set of measures, and a guide through difficult logical passages.
A touchstone is a black stone used to test the purity of gold – similarly, this book tests the soundness of arguments.
2
Locate the prayer for protection
The author asks God to 'protect you from the place of error in the straits of consideration.' This shows that the book is meant as a safeguard – it equips the reader to avoid logical fallacies in subtle or complex arguments.
The phrase 'straits of consideration' refers to narrow, tricky points where reasoning can easily go wrong.
3
Connect to the title 'Mahakk al-Nazar'
The title itself means 'the touchstone of speculation.' The passage explicitly says the book contains 'the editing of the touchstone' (taḥrīr miḥakk al-naẓar). So the entire work is a refined tool for critical examination of arguments.
Think of it as a handbook for logical scrutiny – like a modern textbook on critical thinking but rooted in Islamic intellectual tradition.
4
Understand the purpose statement
The passage transitions with 'Amma ba'd' (to proceed) to state that the truth of judgment concerning prohibition in religion (or a similar matter) is clarified through these scales. The ultimate aim is to preserve the reader from error and enable sound inference.
This establishes that the book is not just abstract logic – it is meant to be applied to religious rulings ('dīn') and theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله حمدا مع كثرته لا ينضو حق حلاله واشكره متظاهرا وان كان مع امتداده لابرازی سمانه والكل من فعله انه لا يكلف عنده من الحمد والشكر الا قدر استطاعته واستفاد له وصلى الله على محمد عبده ورسوله خير خلقه وعزاله اما بعد كان صدق القضائك ابن المنع في الدين حشرنا الله واياك في حملة المعايير وفيه تحرير محك النظر والافتكا وليعصمك من مكان الغلط في مضايق الاعتبار
Praise be to God, praise with its abundance, not exhausted by the rights of His permissible things, and I thank Him outwardly even though with its extension it removes its blemishes, and all is from His act, that He does not require from one of praise and thanks except the extent of his capacity and benefit for him. And may God bless Muhammad, His servant and messenger, the best of His creation and His family. To proceed: The truth of your judgment [concerning] prohibition in religion – may God gather us and you among the bearers of the scales – and in it is the editing of the touchstone of speculation and the opening, and to protect you from the place of error in the straits of consideration.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the book, which is a prayerful introduction typical of classical Islamic scholarly works. The author, Imam al-Ghazali, begins with praise of God, emphasizing that all praise and thanks are by God's grace and within human capacity. He then sends blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad. The phrase 'اما بعد' transitions to the main purpose: addressing the reader (likely a student or colleague) about 'prohibition in religion' – perhaps a reference to avoiding error. The author prays that God gather them among the 'bearers of the scales' (حملة المعايير), meaning those who possess the criteria to distinguish truth from falsehood. The book itself is called 'محك النظر' (the touchstone of speculation), and its aim is to edit or refine this touchstone ('تحرير محك النظر') and to protect the reader from falling into error in difficult points of inference ('مضايق الاعتبار'). Thus, the introduction establishes the epistemological purpose: to provide a logical tool (a scale) to safeguard one's reasoning from mistakes, especially in complex matters where error is common. This sets the stage for the logical discussions that follow.

المعايير
الغلط
مضايق الاعتبار

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali opens his book by declaring it a 'touchstone' – a logical standard to protect reasoning from error in subtle matters of inference.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Opening of Mahakk al-Nazar: The Touchstone of Reasoning
Author presents the book title and begins with basmala and hamdala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the components of a traditional Islamic book opening as exemplified by al-Ghazali's Mahakk al-Nazar.

Core Idea
The basmala and hamdala ground logic in divine gratitude.

This passage opens al-Ghazali's logical treatise 'Mahakk al-Nazar' (The Touchstone of Contemplation). Before delving into syllogisms and fallacies, the author invokes God's name, praises Him, and sends blessings on the Prophet. This is not mere ritual; it signals that logic, when properly used, is a tool for truth-seeking under divine guidance. In this lesson, we examine the structure and significance of this opening.

Guided Path
1
Identify the title
The first line gives the book title: 'محك النظر' (Mahakk al-Nazar). Note that it is attributed to 'الإمام الغزالي'. This establishes the author and subject.
2
Recognize the basmala
The phrase 'بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم' (In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful) is the Islamic invocation that begins most works. It invokes God's mercy and sets a sacred tone.
3
Analyze the hamdala
The phrase 'الحمد لله رب العالمين' (Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds) expresses gratitude and acknowledges God as the ultimate source of knowledge. In logic, this reminds us that all reasoning depends on divine reality.
4
Consider the salawat
The blessing on the Prophet and his family ('الصلوة على عدة خلقه محمد واله اجمعين') connects the work to the prophetic tradition, emphasizing that logic serves the truth brought by Islam.
5
Reflect on the integration
Al-Ghazali deliberately opens a logic book with these religious formulas. This shows that for him, logic is not secular but a tool for understanding God's creation and law.
This contrasts with purely secular approaches to logic.

Mahakk al-Nazar by Imam al-Ghazali. In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to God, Lord of the worlds, and blessings upon the best of His creation, Muhammad and his family all together.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Ghazali's logical work. It starts with the title, followed by the basmala and hamdala, which is the standard Islamic introduction. The author invokes God's name and praises Him, then sends blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad and his family. This sets the spiritual tone for the treatise. The rest of the page (not cleanly captured) likely continues the introduction, possibly explaining the purpose of the book.

Key Takeaway
The opening of an Islamic scholarly work, including logic, typically begins with the basmala and hamdala to affirm the divine foundation of knowledge.

The Basmala as the Gateway to Mahakk al-Nazar
Author begins with basmala and praise, introduction to the work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the basmala and explain its role in framing al-Ghazali's logical work.

Core Idea
Every Islamic scholarly work begins with God's name — even a book on logic.

Imam al-Ghazali opens Mahakk al-Nazar with the basmala, aligning his logical inquiry with divine blessing. This lesson examines that opening invocation as both a pious act and a pedagogical signal.

Guided Path
1
Locate the basmala
Find the phrase 'بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم' in the Arabic text. This is the only certain part of the opening.
The basmala is a fixed formula used to begin any important action in Islam.
2
Understand its function
The basmala is an invocation of God's name, seeking blessing and assistance. In Islamic scholarship, it signals that the work's purpose is ultimately for Allah.
It also serves as a memorization anchor for the text.
3
Connect to the work's aim
Mahakk al-Nazar is a work on logic ('the touchstone of speculation'). The basmala reminds that logic is a tool to serve truth and divine guidance, not an end in itself.
Al-Ghazali integrates logical reasoning with Islamic piety.

Author opens with praise and prayer, then states the book's title and subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the purpose of Mahakk al-Nazar and identify its two key terms.

Core Idea
Mahakk al-Nazar is a touchstone for testing arguments.

The author opens with the traditional Islamic scholarly formula of praise and prayer, then announces the book's title and its goal: to provide the laws of logic as an aid for seekers of truth in distinguishing sound reasoning from unsound. This introduction sets the stage for a work that treats logic as a practical tool, not merely a theoretical discipline.

Guided Path
1

Notice the author begins with 'Alhamdulillah' (Praise to God) and salutations on the Prophet. This is the standard opening of Islamic scholarly works, placing the book within the religious tradition.
2
Identify the book's title
Find the phrase 'Mahakk al-Nazar' in the Arabic. The term 'Mahakk' means 'touchstone' – a tool used to test the purity of metal. Here it symbolizes a tool for testing the validity of arguments.
3
Understand the stated purpose
The author says the book 'encompasses the laws and principles of logic' to help 'the seeker of truth distinguish the correct from the corrupt.' This frames logic as a practical aid for truth-seeking.

Opening Doxology: The Religious Frame of Islamic Logic
Author opens with basmalah, hamdalah, and salawat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three parts of the opening doxology and explain why a classical logic text begins with religious formulas.

Core Idea
A logic book’s opening prayer shows that reason serves revelation.

You may be surprised that a book on logic—a discipline of pure reason—begins with a prayer. But in the Islamic tradition, even the most rational sciences are pursued within the framework of tawhid. The author’s opening doxology is not a mere convention; it announces that this logic is a tool for understanding Allah’s creation and His law. Let’s unpack the three formulas that make up this sacred opening.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basmalah
Locate 'بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم' (In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful). This phrase invokes Allah’s name and seeks His blessing at the start of any significant action.
2
Recognize the hamdalah
Next is 'الحمد لله رب العالمين' (Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds). This praises Allah as the sole source of all goodness and knowledge.
3
Find the salawat
Then 'والصلاة على عبده ورسوله محمد وعلى آله وصحبه وسلم' (Prayer and peace upon His servant and messenger Muhammad and his family and companions). This honors the Prophet through whom revelation came.
4
Connect the doxology to logic's purpose
Understanding that the doxology frames logic as an act of worship. The author signals that logical reasoning is not independent of divine guidance; it is a tool to serve the shari‘ah and to gain knowledge of Allah's signs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم الحمد لله رب العالمين والصلاة على عبده ورسوله محمد وعلى آله وصحبه وسلم [؟]
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds, and prayers and peace be upon His servant and messenger Muhammad and upon his family and companions [؟]
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is the opening doxology of the book. As is customary in Islamic scholarly works, the author begins with the basmalah ('In the name of Allah'), followed by the hamdalah (praise of Allah as Lord of the worlds), and then the salawat (blessings upon the Prophet Muhammad and his family and companions). This establishes the religious context and signals that the subject of logic, though a rational discipline, is pursued within the framework of Islamic theology and with the intention of serving religious knowledge. The phrase 'أما بعد' (amma ba'd) typically follows, introducing the main subject, but due to OCR corruption, the rest of the sentence is not clearly readable.

Introducing Mahakk al-Nazar: Ghazali's Criterion of Reflection
Front matter: title and edition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author, title, and purpose of Ghazali's Mahakk al-Nazar and explain how the title reflects its function as a standard for evaluating reasoning.

Core Idea
Title reveals the book's mission: a criterion to test and validate reasoning.

This lesson examines the front matter of a foundational work in Islamic logic. The title, 'Mahakk al-Nazar,' immediately signals the book's purpose: to provide a touchstone for correct reasoning. Understanding the title and the author's authoritative status sets the stage for appreciating the logical tools he presents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author and his title
Find the name 'Abu Hamid al-Ghazali' and his honorific 'Hujjat al-Islam'. Recognize that this indicates his supreme authority in Islamic scholarship.
2
Analyze the title 'Mahakk al-Nazar'
'Mahakk' means criterion or touchstone, and 'Nazar' means reflection or reasoning. Together, the title promises a standard to judge the correctness of arguments.
3
Note the edition and publication details
Observe that this is a second edition, verified by two scholars and printed in Cairo. This helps with textual authenticity and provenance.
4
Connect the title to the book's role in Islamic logic

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Opening Prayer and Purpose of Mahakk al-Nazar
Author opens with praise and states the purpose of writing the book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why al-Ghazali opens his logical work with a prayer and a critique of taqlid, and identify the epistemological purpose of Mahakk al-Nazar.

Core Idea
Truth requires both critical examination and divine aid; taqlid is the enemy of insight.

Al-Ghazali's opening is not merely a religious formality. He sets the stage for a rigorous logical method by contrasting blind imitation (taqlid) with critical examination (nazar), and he emphasizes that the seeker of truth must combine intellectual effort with sincere supplication. This lesson unpacks the purpose behind these opening words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's stated motivation for writing
Find where al-Ghazali says why he wrote the book. Look for the phrase 'فإن صدق اقتضائك' indicating a sincere request from a brother in faith. This shows the work was a response to a need for a tool against error.
2
Distinguish between taqlid and nazar
Al-Ghazali mentions rising above 'the lowland of imitation' (حضيض التقليد). Taqlid is blind acceptance without verification. Nazar (the touchstone) is the critical examination that protects from error. Understand how these are opposite approaches in seeking truth.
In later Islamic sciences, taqlid becomes a technical term, but here it is general.
3
Analyze the role of supplication (du'a) in the quest for truth
He asks the reader to pray that he be shown truth as truth and falsehood as falsehood. Why is divine guidance necessary? The passage suggests that without God's help, even rigorous logic can lead to vanity ('هباء').
This integrates spiritual sincerity with intellectual effort.
4
Synthesize the purpose of the book
Combine the elements: the book is a touchstone (miḥakk) for theoretical examination, designed to train the mind to discern truth from falsehood. But it must be accompanied by reliance on God, as the journey is long and the pitfalls many.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم. قال الشيخ الإمام حجة الإسلام محمد بن محمد بن محمد الغزالي رحمة الله عليه: أحمد الله حمداً كثيراً متواتراً وإن كان مع كثرته لا يقضي حق جلاله، وأشكره شكراً مديداً ظاهراً وإن كان امتداده لا يوازي محالب أفاضله، وأتكل على فضله إنه لا يكاف عبده من الحمد والشكر إلا قدر استطاعته واستقلاله، وصلى الله على محمد عبده ورسوله خير خلقه وعلى آله. أما بعد: فإن صدق اقتضائك أيها الأخ في الدين – حشرنا الله وإياك في جملة المتحابين – وفية تحرير محك النظر والانتقاد يعصمك عن مكامن الغلط في إتمام مضائق الاعتبار، قدمي غب الانقباض، وبعث في نفسي داعية الانتهاض، وحواني إلى فن أفرحته بحكم المشادة والفجر، عدت إليه معاودة من التفت إلى ما هجر، وثقل الالتفات إلى ما هجر ثقيل. ولكن نفاسة الثمن بنفاسة اليمن كفيل، ولا ثمن إلا ما أرجوه من بركة دعائك عند صدق رجائك، وذلك في خلواتك عند اعتياد صلواتك. فإني لم أنتبع فيها انترحته هواك إلا مقترباً إلى رضاء الله تعالى برضاك. فانا مقسم عليك بما تشاء من أخوة الدين أن لا تنساني في الدعاء، وأن تستشرك فيه من يعرفك من صلحاء الأصدقاء. فلم يبق إلا دعوات أهل الصلاح، فقد أتى الصادق المصدوق صلوات الله عليه أن الدعاء للمؤمن عدة وسلاح. فإن لم يكن الدعاء هو المعول – فعلى ماذا تشكل؟ فالأمر والخطاب جد، والسفر طويل والزاد قليل، والشأن خطير والعمر قصير، وفي العمل تقصير، والإضافة زلاة، والحاضر من النقد زيف والناقد مغير، ولكن الجود غزير والرب قدير، وأضل الله بالكحول جدير. فإن أقال عشر أننا بدعاء مسلم واحد فما ذلك على الله بعزيز. وها أنا أفرح عليك أن تقول في دعائك: اللهم أره الحق حقاً وارزقه اتباعه، وأره الباطل باطلاً وارزقه اجتنابه. وما أحوج إلى هذا من ركب متن الخطر في الارتفاع عن حضيض التقليد مع سلامة ته إلى يفاع الاطلاع والاستبصار مع خطر عاقبته وتفاقم غائله. فإن لم يره الحق حقاً كان نظاره كله هباء، وإن لم يوفقه.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The Shaykh, the Imam, the Proof of Islam, Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, said: I praise Allah with abundant and continuous praise, even if its abundance does not fulfill the right of His majesty, and I thank Him with manifest and extended thanks, even if its extension does not equal the demands of His favors, and I rely on His bounty, for His servant cannot sufficiently praise and thank Him except to the extent of his ability and self-sufficiency. And may Allah bless Muhammad, His servant and messenger, the best of His creation, and his family. To proceed: Indeed, the sincerity of your request, O brother in religion—may Allah gather us and you among those who love each other—and the zeal in writing a precise examination of theoretical insight and criticism will protect you from the hidden places of error in traversing the narrow paths of consideration. It prompted me, after reluctance, and aroused in me the motive to rise, and urged me to an art that I had delighted in by virtue of severe competition and dawn; I returned to it as one who turns to what he had abandoned, and turning to what was abandoned is burdensome. But the preciousness of the price guarantees the preciousness of the gem, and there is no price except what I hope for from the blessing of your supplication in truth of your hope, and that in your private moments during your constant prayers. For I have not pursued therein what you desired except as a means of drawing near to Allah's pleasure through your pleasure. So I adjure you by whatever brotherhood of religion you wish that you do not forget me in supplication, and that you include with you those whom you know among righteous friends. For nothing remains but the prayers of the righteous; indeed, the truthful and believed one, peace be upon him, said that supplication for a believer is a weapon and a shield. If supplication is not the mainstay, then on what can one rely? The matter and discourse are serious, the journey is long and the provision is little, the affair is critical and life is short, and in action there is deficiency, and the addition is a slip, and the present coin is counterfeit and the critic is changing, but generosity is abundant and the Lord is powerful, and Allah is fit to lead astray those who are intoxicated. If He accepts ten from us through the supplication of a single Muslim, that is not difficult for Allah. And I enjoin you to say in your supplication: O Allah, show him the truth as truth and grant him its following, and show him falsehood as falsehood and grant him its avoidance. How much more in need of this is one who mounts the dangerous path of rising above the lowland of imitation, with his safety leading to the height of insight and discernment, while the danger of its outcome and the severity of its evil consequence are great. For if he is not shown the truth as truth, all his contemplation will be vain, and if He does not grant him success.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This is the opening of al-Ghazali's Mahakk Nazar. It begins with the basmala and a traditional praise of Allah and blessings on the Prophet. The author then explains the motivation behind writing the book: a sincere request from a brother in faith. The passage highlights the importance of sincere supplication and the pursuit of truth free from imitation (taqlid). Al-Ghazali emphasizes that the quest for knowledge is a serious endeavor with a long journey, short life, and many pitfalls. He asks the reader to pray for him, specifically that he may be shown the truth as truth and falsehood as falsehood. This sets the tone for the work as a tool for critical examination (mahakk al-nazar) to protect from error. The style is highly rhetorical and emotional, reflecting the author's spiritual concern.

Key Terms
محك النظر
تقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Poverty and ignorance
B) Hidden places of error
C) Difficult theological debates
D) Lack of prayer

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali opens Mahakk al-Nazar by grounding the pursuit of truth in both critical thinking and sincere supplication, positioning the work as a tool to escape the pitfalls of blind imitation.

Introduction and Front Matter

Understanding al-Ghazali's Conclusion and Advice
Author concludes by summarizing the book's value and urging the reader to pray.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relationship between Mahakk Nazar and Mi'yar al-'Ulum and explain the importance of sincerity in seeking knowledge as emphasized in this passage.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's conclusion reveals his meticulous care for knowledge and the primacy of ikhlas (sincerity).

In this concluding passage, al-Ghazali provides a window into his methodology: he summarizes lofty principles in a small book, promises a more detailed work, and urgently reminds us that knowledge must be sought purely for Allah. This lesson helps you appreciate how the great scholar balanced brevity with depth and how he integrated intellectual rigor with spiritual devotion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two great principles
Al-Ghazali states that his small book sets in motion 'two great principles' (أصلان عظيمان). Though he does not name them explicitly, from the context of the book they are likely the principles of logic and the principles of verification (the tools for distinguishing truth from falsehood).
These principles are the foundation of the entire work.
2
Examine the relationship with Mi'yar al-'Ulum
Al-Ghazali reveals that Mahakk Nazar is a summary, and the detailed exposition exists in his larger work Mi'yar al-'Ulum. However, he states that the latter is not circulated because it needs refinement (تهذيب وتنقيح). This shows his commitment to precision and his awareness of the need for continuous improvement.
Mi'yar al-'Ulum is a more advanced logical work; Mahakk Nazar serves as an accessible introduction.
3
Analyze the ethical counsel
Al-Ghazali renews his advice to the reader to persist in supplication (الدعاء) and to seek knowledge with pure intention for Allah's sake. He condemns those who call others to Allah while themselves being turned away. This underscores that logic and knowledge are not ends in themselves but means to draw closer to Allah.
The ethical dimension is integral to Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحق به أن قال: اعلم أن القدر الذي حررته في هذا الكتاب مع صغر حجمه حَرَّكتُ به أصولاً عظيمتين. أمعنت في تفهم الكتاب تشرفت إلى مزيد إيضاح في بعض ما أجملته، واشتغلت الحكم الحال عن تفصيله. وذلك التفصيل قد دوَّنته في كتاب معيار العلوم، إلا أني لم أفش تلك النسخة ولم تداولها الأيدي بعد، لأنها كانت مفتقرة إلى مزيد تهذيب وتنقيح بحذف وزيادة وتحريف، وقد دفعت الأقدار دون تهذيبها. فإن استأخر الأجل وانحسرت العوائق، انصرفت إليه الجمعة وانقطعت على عمارته بتهذيب ما يجب أن يذيَّب صادقت فيه ما أعوزك في هذا الكتاب. وأنا الآن مجدد وصيتك بالمواظبة على الدعاء، سائلاً من الله تعالى أن يصلح بيتي فيما أتعاطاه من تصنيف كتاب وإفادة علم، أُقصد به خالص وجه الله، غير مزوج بحفظ من حظوظ الدنيا. وما أخس حال من مهدَّد مناقب سبيل الآخرة وهو إلى الدنيا ملتفت، يدعو الخلق إلى الله وهو عنه معرض! فنعوذ بالله من شرور أنفسنا وسيئات أعمالنا، ونسأل الله تعالى إصلاح أحوالنا وأقوالنا وأفعالنا، فهو ولي الإجابة بفضله وسعة جوده. والحمد لله رب العالمين.
Then he appended to it and said: Know that the amount I have written in this book, despite its small size, has set in motion two great principles. I have delved deeply into understanding the book, and I became honored by additional clarification of some matters that I had summarized, and the ruling of the present situation prevented me from detailing them. That detail I have recorded in the book 'Mi'yar al-'Ulum', but I did not disseminate that copy nor did it circulate among hands yet, because it was in need of further refinement and revision by deletion, addition, and correction, and circumstances prevented its refinement. If the term of life is extended and obstacles are removed, I will turn to it on Friday and devote myself to its construction by refining what must be refined, and I will verify what you lacked in this book. And now I renew my advice to you to persist in supplication, asking Allah Almighty to rectify my situation in what I undertake of authoring books and imparting knowledge, by which I intend the pure face of Allah, not mixed with preservation of worldly shares. How base is the state of one who, while threatening the paths of the Hereafter, is turned towards the world, calling people to Allah while he himself is turned away from Him! So we seek refuge in Allah from the evils of our souls and the evil of our deeds, and we ask Allah Almighty to rectify our states, our words, and our deeds, for He is the protector of answering by His bounty and the vastness of His generosity. And praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This is the concluding section of Mahakk Nazar, where al-Ghazali provides a meta-commentary on his own work. He reveals that this small book encapsulates two major principles (probably logic and theology), and that a more detailed treatment exists in his larger work 'Mi'yar al-'Ulum' (The Criterion of Sciences). He explains that the latter was not widely circulated because it needed further editing. He promises to refine it if circumstances permit. He then exhorts the reader to persist in supplication, and makes a heartfelt prayer for himself and the reader, emphasizing sincerity in seeking knowledge for Allah's sake alone. The passage ends with a standard closing formula. It reflects al-Ghazali's concern for the purity of intention and the integration of knowledge and practice.

Key Terms
معيار العلوم
أصلان عظيمان

The Spiritual Foundation of Logic
Author opens with praise, address to reader, and supplication for guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements of al-Ghazali's opening address and explain the role of supplication in the pursuit of knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge without divine guidance leads to error; al-Ghazali opens with supplication for protection from falsehood.

Before delving into the technicalities of logic, al-Ghazali sets the tone by establishing the spiritual foundation. This opening is not mere formality; it teaches us that the seeker of truth must begin with humility and reliance on God. The author emphasizes that sincere prayer is a weapon and a provision for the believer, especially for those who seek to distinguish truth from falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author and book title
Note the full name: Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Ghazali. The book is titled 'Mihakk al-Nazar' (The Touchstone of Speculation), which indicates its purpose as a tool for testing reasoning.
Al-Ghazali is known as 'Hujjat al-Islam' (Proof of Islam).
2
Understand the occasion for writing
Al-Ghazali explains that a 'brother in religion' requested this work to protect him from errors in speculative thought. Despite his initial reluctance, he was compelled to write due to the sincerity of the request.
This shows the importance of sincere intention (niyya) in scholarly work.
3
Recognize the spiritual admonition
Al-Ghazali stresses the need for prayer and supplication, citing a hadith that 'prayer for the believer is a weapon and a provision'. He asks the reader to pray for him, as the journey of knowledge is long and fraught with danger.
The phrase 'the present coin is counterfeit' refers to the prevalence of falsehood and the need for discernment.
4
Analyze the prayer for guidance
The specific supplication is: 'O God, show him the truth as truth and grant him its following, and show him falsehood as falsehood and grant him its avoidance.' This encapsulates the goal of the book—enabling one to distinguish truth from falsehood.
This prayer is a model for all seekers of knowledge, especially in logic and theology.

In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The Shaykh, the Imam, the Proof of Islam, Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Ghazali, may God's mercy be upon him, said: I praise God with abundant, frequent praise, even if its abundance does not fulfill the right of His majesty, and I thank Him with prolonged thanks, outwardly and inwardly, even if its prolongation does not equal the objects of His favors. I rely on His bounty, for His servant cannot fulfill the praise and thanks except to the extent of his ability and capacity. And may God bless Muhammad, His servant and messenger, the best of His creation, and his family. To proceed: Indeed, the truth of your request, O brother in religion (may God gather us and you among those who love each other), and [your request] for the composition of 'The Touchstone of Speculation and Critique' to protect you from the traps of error in completing the narrow paths of consideration, compelled me after reluctance and stirred in me the motivation to rise and drove me to a craft that I had abandoned due to the difficulty of disputation and the dawn [of renewed effort]. I returned to it like one who turns to what he had abandoned, and turning to what is abandoned is heavy. But the preciousness of the price guarantees the preciousness of the Yemen [trade], and there is no price except what I hope from the blessing of your prayer at the sincerity of your hope, and that in your seclusions during your supererogatory prayers. For I did not follow in it what I chose for your desire except seeking nearness to God's pleasure through your pleasure. So I adjure you by whatever you wish of the brotherhood of religion not to forget me in prayer and to include in it those righteous friends you know. Nothing remains but the prayers of the righteous. Indeed, the truthful and believed (peace be upon him) said: Prayer for the believer is a weapon and a provision. If prayer is not the main support, then on what can one rely? The matter and the end are serious, the journey is long, the provision is little, the affair is grave, life is short, in action there is shortcoming, the light [of guidance] is hidden, the present coin is counterfeit, the critic is perplexed, but generosity is abundant, the Lord is powerful, and God is worthy of helping with drunkenness [i.e., heedlessness]. If He accepts from us the prayer of one Muslim, that is not difficult for God. And here I urge you to say in your prayer: O God, show him the truth as truth and grant him its following, and show him falsehood as falsehood and grant him its avoidance. How much more needful of this is one who mounts the danger of rising from the lowland of imitation with safety to the height of insight and vision, with the danger of its consequence and the gravity of its evil result. For if he does not see the truth as truth, his entire consideration becomes vain, and if he is not granted success...
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the book 'Mahakk al-Nazar'. It begins with the basmala and praise of God, followed by the author's identification as Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Ghazali. The author then addresses the reader as a brother in religion, explaining that the reader's request for a work on 'Mihakk al-Nazar wa al-Intikar' (The Touchstone of Speculation and Critique) compelled him to write despite his initial reluctance. He emphasizes the importance of sincere prayer and asks the reader to pray for him to be shown truth and protected from falsehood. The tone is deeply spiritual, highlighting the gravity of seeking knowledge and the need for divine assistance. The passage sets the stage for the technical logical discussions to follow.

The Spiritual Foundation of Logical Study
Author provides closing remarks on the book's content and its relation to Ma'yar al-Ilm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relationship between this book and Ma'yar al-Ilm, and explain why al-Ghazali emphasizes sincerity of intention in logical study.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's logical works are incomplete without sincerity and reliance on Allah.

In this concluding passage, al-Ghazali does not simply end his book; he reveals the ethical foundation of his entire logical project. He connects the concise principles of this volume to a larger, unfinished work (Ma'yar al-Ilm) and reminds us that true knowledge requires purification of intention. As we learn the tools of logic, we must also internalize the adab (etiquette) of seeking knowledge solely for Allah's sake.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two books mentioned
Al-Ghazali refers to two works: this concise book and 'Ma'yar al-Ilm'. Locate them in the Arabic text and note how he contrasts their scope and completion status.
'Ma'yar al-Ilm' is the detailed expansion.
2
Understand their relationship
He says the detail of what he summarized here is already contained in Ma'yar al-Ilm. So this book is a concise summary; Ma'yar is the detailed commentary. Recognize this pattern of summary followed by expansion in Islamic scholarly writing.
3
Analyze the obstacles to completion
Al-Ghazali mentions that Ma'yar was not circulated because it needed revision, and circumstances (time, health?) prevented finishing it. Reflect on how even great scholars face practical limitations.
4
Extract the ethical imperative
He renews his advice to pray for sincerity, warning against hypocrisy. The pursuit of logic must be for Allah's face alone. This is a core principle of Islamic education (adab al-'ilm).
Shows that logical tools without right intention can lead to arrogance or misuse.

Conclusion of the book. Then append to it that he said: Know that the amount I have written in this book, despite its small size, moves two great principles. If you delve into understanding the book, you become honored to seek more clarification in some of what I have summarized, and the preoccupation of the current situation prevented its detail. And that detail I have already compacted in the book 'Ma'yar al-Ilm'. However, I have not disseminated that copy, nor has it been circulated except in my hand, because it needed more refinement and revision by deletion, addition, and correction. And circumstances prevented its refinement. If the term of life is extended and obstacles are removed, and I turn to it with full attention and devote myself to its construction with refinement of what must be polished, I will accomplish what you need in this book. And now I renew my advice to you to persevere in prayer, asking God Almighty to make my intention in composing this book and offering benefit purely for His face, not mixed with preservation of worldly fortunes. How base is the state of one who paves the way for the Hereafter while inclining to the world, calls creation to God while turning away from Him! So we seek refuge in God from the evils of our souls and the badness of our deeds, and we ask God Almighty to rectify our states, our words, and our actions. He is the one who answers by His bounty and generosity. And praise be to God, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is the concluding section of the book. The author, al-Ghazali, explains that this small book encapsulates two great principles. He promises further elaboration in his larger work 'Ma'yar al-Ilm' (The Standard of Knowledge), but notes that this work is not yet ready for public circulation because it requires further editing. He expresses his intention to refine it if time permits. He then renews his plea for the reader's prayers, emphasizing that his sole aim is sincerity for God's sake, not worldly gain. He ends with a powerful warning against hypocrisy and a prayer for rectification of one's state, words, and deeds. This conclusion underscores the ethical and spiritual dimensions of the logical project.

Key Terms
معيار العلوم (mi'yar al-'ulum): Title of another work by al-Ghazali, 'The Standard of Knowledge', which expands on the principles of this book. [Note: No significant shift – this is al-Ghazali's own reference to his other book.]

Parsing the Title Page of Mahakk al-Nazar
Title page of Mahakk al-Nazar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author, his honorifics, and the significance of the title of Mahakk al-Nazar from its title page.

Core Idea
A title page is a statement of authority and purpose in Islamic scholarship.

When studying an Islamic text, the title page provides crucial information about the author and the work's standing. This lesson guides you through the title page of al-Ghazali's logic work, Mahakk al-Nazar, helping you recognize key elements such as the author's name, honorifics, and the work's title. You will learn to parse classical Arabic honorifics and understand their significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the title of the work
Look at the first line: 'مِحَ النَّظر'. Despite the OCR error (the correct title is 'محك النظر'), this is the name of the book. Note that 'Mahakk' means 'touchstone' and 'al-Nazar' means 'contemplation' or 'reasoning'.
The title is given without diacritics in the original.
2
Find the author's name
Scan the subsequent lines for the author's full name. The text reads: 'تأليف الإِمَامِ المجددِ ، حُجَّةِ الإِسْلَام وَالمَسَلمِينَ زين الدين ، أبو حامد محمد بن محمد بن محمد بن أحمد الغزالي القوسي الطابراني الشافعي'. Note that the OCR has 'أن حامد' but it should be 'أبو حامد'.
The author's lineage shows he is from Tus (الغزالي) and his school is Shafi'i.
3
Parse the honorifics
The honorifics include 'الإمام المجدد' (the Imam, the renewer), 'حجة الإسلام' (the Proof of Islam), and 'زين الدين' (Ornament of the Religion). These indicate al-Ghazali's high scholarly status.
These honorifics reflect his role in reviving Islamic thought.
4
Note the birth-death dates
The title page includes al-Ghazali's lifespan: '٤٥٠-٥٠٥هـ'. This corresponds to 1058-1111 CE. This helps contextualize the work historically.
The Hijri dates are given in Arabic numerals.

مِحَ النَّظر
تأليف الإِمَامِ المجددِ ، حُجَّةِ الإِسْلَام وَالمَسامِينَ زيز الدين ، أن حامد
محمد بن محمد بن مُحَمَّدِ بْنِ أَحْمَدَ الغَزَالي القُوسِيّ الطَابَرَانِي الشَّافِعِي رَضِي اللَّهُ عَنْهُ

The Touchstone of Contemplation. Composition of the Imam, the Renewer, the Proof of Islam and the Muslims, Zain al-Din Abu Hamid Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Ghazali al-Tusi al-Tabarani al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him (450-505 AH). 47
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is the title page of al-Ghazali's work on logic, Mahakk al-Nazar (The Touchstone of Contemplation). It provides the full name of the author: Abu Hamid Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Ghazali, along with his honorifics: al-Imam (the leader), al-Mujaddid (the renewer of the faith), Hujjat al-Islam (the proof of Islam), and Zain al-Din (ornament of the religion). The work was composed by al-Ghazali, one of the most influential scholars in Islamic history, known for his expertise in theology, philosophy, Sufism, and logic. The date 450-505 AH corresponds to his lifespan (1058-1111 CE). This page introduces the text as a contribution to the field of logic from an Islamic perspective. Students should note that al-Ghazali's approach to logic integrates Islamic principles with Aristotelian traditions.

Identifying the Author and Title of Mahakk al-Nazar
Title page presenting the book name and author's full lineage and honorifics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a classical Islamic author from his full name, nisbahs, and honorifics as presented on a title page.

Core Idea
A title page condenses an author's scholarly identity into a few lines.

Before diving into al-Ghazali's logical treatise Mahakk al-Nazar, we first encounter its title page. This page is not mere decoration; it tells you who the author is, what school he follows, and his standing in the Islamic tradition. Learning to read such a page is a basic skill for any student of Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the title
Look for the Arabic phrase 'مِحَكُّ النَّظَرِ'. Note its meaning: 'The Touchstone of Reasoning'.
2
Identify the author's name
Find the full name: Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Ghazali. Recognize the patronymic chain.
3
Parse the nisbahs
al-Tusi refers to the city Tus; al-Tabrani may refer to a quarter; al-Shafi'i indicates his legal school.
4
Note the honorifics
'Hujjat al-Islam' (Proof of Islam) and 'Imam al-Mujaddid' (Renewing Imam) signal his authority.
5
Confirm the dates

Introduction and Front Matter
