The Occasion of Mahakk al-Nazar: A Revival from Reluctance
Author's preface explaining the reason for returning to logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the purpose and occasion of al-Ghazali's Mahakk al-Nazar from its preface.

Core Idea
A brother's sincere request revived al-Ghazali's commitment to logic after a period of abandonment.

This opening passage of Mahakk al-Nazar is not mere formality. It reveals al-Ghazali's personal journey: he had abandoned logic out of weariness, but a fellow believer's request stirred him to return. This sets the stage for a work that aims to safeguard reasoning from error. Understanding this context helps us appreciate the work's intent and al-Ghazali's own evolution as a thinker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conventional elements
Locate the basmalah, hamdalah (praise), and salawat (blessings on the Prophet). These are standard Islamic prefatory formulas. Notice that al-Ghazali begins with 'bismillah' and 'ahmadullah'—this aligns with the genre of Islamic scholarly works.
This section also includes a prayer for himself and his reader.
2
Find the addressee and the request
After 'amma ba‘d', al-Ghazali addresses 'O brother in faith' and mentions a 'sincere request' to produce a work on 'the measure of reflection and thought' (taḥrīr maḥall al-naẓar wal-iftikār). This is the direct occasion for the book.
The phrase 'taḥrīr maḥall al-naẓar' is the title's inspiration.
3
Analyze al-Ghazali's emotional state
He says the request 'broke from me the edge of reluctance' and 'aroused in me the motive for rising'. Earlier, he had abandoned 'an art' (logic) out of 'weariness and boredom'. The request transformed his attitude.
This shows al-Ghazali's personal struggle with the subject.
4
Identify the purpose of the work
The book is meant to 'protect you from the hidden places of error in the narrow passages of consideration'. That is, it safeguards the reader from fallacies in reasoning. This defines the work's goal: a logical guide against error.
‘Isma (protection) is a key term here.
5
Connect to the broader intellectual context
Al-Ghazali had previously engaged with logic in works like Mi‘yar al-‘Ilm and al-Mustasfa. This preface suggests he returned to the subject despite personal fatigue, driven by a fellow Muslim's need. This reflects the communal dimension of Islamic scholarship.
Understanding this helps date the work in his career.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم وبه ثقتي قال الشيخ الإمام حجة الإسلام محمد بن محمد الغزالي الطوسي رحمة الله عليه أحمد الله حمدا كثيرا متواترا وإن كان مع كثرته لا يقضي حق جلاله وأشكره شكرا مديدا متظاهرا وإن كان مع امتداده لا يوازي سحائب إفضاله وأتكل على فضله إنه لا يكلف عبده من الحمد والشكر إلا قدر استطاعته واستقلاله وأصلي على عبده ورسوله محمد خير خلقه وعلى آله أما بعد فإن صدق اقتضائك أيها الأخ في الدين حشرنا الله وإياك في زمرة المتحابين فيه تحرير محل النظر والافتكار ليعصمك عن مكامن الغلط في مضايق الاعتبار فل مني غرب الانقباض وبعث في نفسي داعية الانتهاض وحولني إلى فن اطرحته بحكم السآمة والضجر فعدت إليه معاودة من التفت إلى الداعية لتحرير هذا التأليف
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. And in Him is my trust. The Imam, the Proof of Islam, Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Ghazali al-Tusi, may Allah's mercy be upon him, said: I praise Allah with abundant and successive praise, even if its abundance does not fulfill the right of His majesty; and I thank Him with extended and manifest thanks, even if its extension does not equal the clouds of His bounty; and I rely on His favor, for He does not burden His servant with praise and thanks except to the extent of his capacity and ability. And I send blessings upon His servant and messenger Muhammad, the best of His creation, and upon his family. To proceed: Indeed, the sincerity of your request – O brother in faith, may Allah gather us and you among the group of those who love one another for His sake – to produce a work on the measure of reflection and thought, to protect you from the hidden places of error in the narrow passages of consideration, has broken from me the edge of reluctance, and aroused in me the motive for rising, and turned me toward an art that I had abandoned due to weariness and boredom, so I returned to it like one who responds to the call to produce this work.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface (khutba) to Mahakk al-Nazar. It begins with the basmalah, hamdalah, and salawat, as is customary. Then al-Ghazali addresses a specific brother in faith who requested him to write a work on logic (the 'measure of reflection') to safeguard against errors in reasoning. Al-Ghazali states that this request broke his reluctance, aroused his motivation, and turned him back to an art (logic) that he had abandoned out of weariness. He returns to it willingly, as if answering a call. This preface establishes the occasion for the work and al-Ghazali's personal connection to the subject.

Introduction and Front Matter

Al-Ghazali's Motivation: Logic as a Spiritual Act
Author explains his reluctance and motivation to write at a brother's request.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Ghazali’s spiritual and interpersonal motivations for writing a logical treatise, and articulate why he embeds logical study in a framework of divine reliance.

Core Idea
For al-Ghazali, logic is not just a tool—it is an act of worship sustained by prayer.

This preface introduces al-Ghazali's Mahakk al-Nazar, a work on logic. But before any technical discussion, al-Ghazali grounds his project in a spiritual context. He responds to a brother’s request, and frames his return to an abandoned art as a means to seek God’s pleasure through the brother’s prayers. This is not a mere rhetorical flourish; it reveals his view that sound reasoning depends on divine aid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the occasion for the work
Notice that a brother in faith requested a treatise on 'the place of investigation and reflection' (تحرير محل النظر والافتكار) to guard against error. This request is the immediate cause for al-Ghazali to write.
The brother is seeking logical tools to avoid hidden errors in reasoning.
2
Analyze al-Ghazali's emotional state
He mentions that the request broke his reluctance (الانقباض) and stirred him to rise, turning him back to an art he had abandoned out of boredom and vexation (السآمة والضجر). This shows his initial hesitation.
Al-Ghazali had previously left this field, but the brother's request reignites his engagement.
3
Understand the exchange: price and reward
He compares the work to a precious commodity where the high price reflects the value of what is purchased. The only price he seeks is the blessing of the brother's sincere prayers, especially after prayers. This reveals that his motivation is spiritual, not material.
He explicitly says he sought nothing except to draw near to God through the brother's pleasure.
4
Observe the broader human condition
Al-Ghazali reflects on the gravity of life: the journey is long, provision little, life short, actions deficient. He emphasizes reliance on God and the prayers of the righteous. This contextualizes logic within a larger struggle, where human effort is insufficient without divine aid.
He cites the hadith that prayer is a believer's weapon and provision.
5
Identify the proposed supplication
He ends by proposing that the brother pray: 'O God, show him the truth.' This encapsulates that the ultimate goal of logical investigation is to see truth, and that is sought through divine guidance, not mere intellect.
This supplication underscores the integration of logic with spirituality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم وبه ثقتي. قال الشيخ الإمام، حجة الإسلام، محمد بن محمد الغزالي الطوسي رحمة الله عليه: أحمد الله حمداً كثيراً متواتراً وإن كان مع كثرته لا يقضي حق جلاله، وأشكره شكراً مديداً متظاهراً وإن كان مع امتداده لا يوازي سحائب إفضاله، وأتكل على فضله إنه لا يكلف عبده من الحمد والشكر إلا قدر استطاعته واستقلاله، وأصلي على عبده ورسوله محمد خير خلقه وعلى آله. أما بعد: فإن صدق اقتضائك - أيها الأخ في الدين، حشرنا الله وإياك في زمرة المتحابين فيه - تحرير محل النظر والافتكار؛ ليعصمك عن مكامن الغلط في مضايق الاعتبار .. فل مني غرب الانقباض، وبعث في نفسي داعية الانتهاض، وحولني إلى فن اطرحته بحكم السآمة والضجر، فعدت إليه معاودة من التفت إلى ما هجر، وقيل: الالتفات إلى ما هجر ثقيل، ولكن نفاسة الثمن بنفاسة المثمن كفيل، ولا ثمن إلا ما أرجوه من بركة دعائك عند صدق رجائك؛ وذلك في خلواتك، عند أعقاب صلواتك؛ فإني لم أبتغ فيما اقترحته هواك، إلا متقرباً إلى رضا الله تعالى برضاك، وأنا مقسم عليك بما بيننا من أخوة الدين: ألا تنساني في الدعاء، وأن تستشرك فيه من تعرفه من صلحاء الأصدقاء، فلم يبق معول سوى دعوات أهل الصلاح؛ فقد أنب الصادق المصدوق صلوات الله عليه: أن الدعاء للمؤمن عدة وسلاح، فإن لم يكن الدعاء هو المعول .. فعلى ماذا نتكل؟! فالأمر إذ، والخطب جد، والسفر طويل، والزاد قليل، والشأن خطير، والعمر قصير، وفي العمل تقصير، والبضاعة مزجاة، والحاضر من النقد زيف، والناقد بصير، ولكن الجود غزيز، والرب قدير، وفضل الله بالشمول جدير، فإن أقال عثراتنا بدعاء مسلم واحد .. فما ذلك على الله بعسير. وهأنا مقترح عليك أن تقول في دعائك: اللهم؛ أره الحق.
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, and in Him is my trust. The Shaykh, the Imam, the Proof of Islam, Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Ghazali al-Tusi, may God's mercy be upon him, said: I praise God with abundant and continuous praise, even if with its abundance it does not fulfill the right of His majesty, and I thank Him with prolonged and manifest thanks, even if with its prolongation it does not match the clouds of His bounty. And I rely on His bounty; indeed He does not burden His servant with praise and thanks except to the extent of his capacity and ability. And I send blessings upon His servant and messenger Muhammad, the best of His creation, and upon his family. To proceed: The truth of your request, O brother in religion—may God gather us and you in the group of those who love each other in Him—to write a treatise on the place of investigation and reflection (تحرير محل النظر والافتكار) to protect you from the lurking places of error in the narrow passages of consideration, has broken from me the edge of reluctance, and aroused in my soul the motive for rising, and turned me to an art which I had abandoned out of boredom and vexation. So I returned to it like one who turns back to what he had deserted. And it is said: turning to what has been abandoned is burdensome, but the preciousness of the price is a guarantee of the preciousness of the object sold. And there is no price except what I hope for from the blessing of your prayer upon the truth of your hope—and that in your private moments, after your prayers. For I sought nothing in what I proposed of your desire except to draw near to the pleasure of God Almighty through your pleasure. And I adjure you by the brotherhood of religion between us: do not forget me in your prayers, and include in them whoever you know among righteous friends. For no reliance remains except the prayers of the righteous; indeed the truthful and believed one (peace be upon him) informed us that prayer for the believer is a provision and a weapon. So if prayer is not the reliance, then upon what do we rely?! So the matter is grievous, the affair serious, the journey long, the provision little, the matter grave, the life short, and in action there is deficiency, the merchandise is worthless, the ready cash is counterfeit, and the critic is penetrating. But generosity is abundant, the Lord is powerful, and the bounty of God is worthy of encompassing. So if He covers our stumbles through the prayer of one Muslim, that is not difficult for God. And I propose to you that you say in your prayer: O God, show him the truth.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface to his work 'Mahakk al-Nazar'. Al-Ghazali opens with the basmala and praise of God and blessings on the Prophet. He then addresses a brother in faith who requested a treatise on logical investigation (تحرير محل النظر والافتكار) to guard against error. Al-Ghazali describes how this request broke his reluctance and stirred him to return to a field he had abandoned out of boredom. He underscores the value of the work by comparing it to a precious commodity: the high price reflects the value of what is purchased. The only price he seeks is the blessing of the requester's sincere prayers, especially after prayers. He emphasizes that he undertook this only to please God through pleasing the brother. He then adjures the brother to pray for him and includes other righteous friends in those prayers, citing the hadith that prayer is the believer's weapon and provision. He reflects on the gravity of the human condition: a long journey with little provision, short life, flawed actions, and deficient merchandise, but with hope in God's abundant generosity. The preface culminates in a proposed supplication: 'O God, show him the truth.' This sets a spiritual tone for the logical work that follows, grounding the pursuit of sound reasoning in dependence on God and the support of righteous prayers.

Key Terms
محل النظر والافتكار
الغلط
الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Seeking Truth Supplication: Asking Allah to See Haqq and Batil Clearly
Author presents a supplication for guidance and reflects on the difficulty of attaining truth without divine help

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two requests in the author's supplication and explain how each request addresses a specific cognitive obstacle.

Core Idea
Seeing truth and falsehood correctly is a divine gift, not a purely human achievement.

The author opens his logic text not with definitions but with a supplication. This is no accident. He wants the student to recognize that even the most rigorous intellectual tools depend on divine guidance for their proper use. This lesson unpacks that famous dua and its foundational role.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dua's two requests
Read the Arabic from 'اللهم أره الحق حقاً' to 'وارزقه اجتنابه'. Note that the dua has two parts: seeing truth as truth and following it, and seeing falsehood as falsehood and avoiding it.
The dua is comprehensive: it covers both cognition and action.
2
Understand why the dua precedes a logic text
The author places this supplication at the beginning of his book on logic. This shows that logic, while essential, cannot guarantee correct judgment without divine illumination. The dua humbles the seeker.
This is a key feature of Islamic epistemology: intellect and prayer are complementary.
3
Connect each request to a cognitive condition
The author warns: if Allah does not show the truth as truth, 'his entire vision is vain.' And if he is not enabled to act on knowledge, his effort is toil. The dua directly counters these two failures.
Without divine aid, even rigorous reasoning can lead to error or useless learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما هجر ، وقيل : الالتفات إلى ما هُجِرَ ثقيل ، ولكنَّ نفاسة الثمن بنفاسةِ المُثْمَنِ كفيل ، ولا ثمنَ إِلَّا ما أرجوه من بركة دعائِكَ عند صدق رجائِكَ ؛ وذلكَ في خلواتِكَ ، عند أعقاب صلواتِكَ ؛ فإنّي لم أبتغ فيما اقترحته هواكَ ، إلَّا متقرباً إلى رضا الله تعالى برضاك ، وأنا مقسم عليك بما بيننا من أخوة الدين : ألا تنساني في الدعاء ، وأن تستشركَ فيهِ مَنْ تعرفُهُ مِنْ صلحاء الأصدقاء ، فلمْ يبق معوّل سوى دعوات أهل الصلاح ؛ فقد أنب الصادق المصدوق صلوات الله عليهِ : أَنَّ الدعاء للمؤمن عُدَّةٌ وسلاح ، فإن لم يكن الدعاءُ هوَ المعوّلَ .. فعلى ماذا نتّكلُ ؟! فالأمرُ إِذْ ، والخطب جد ، والسفر طويل ، والزاد قليل ، والشأن خطير ، والعمر قصير ، وفي العمل تقصير ، والبضاعة مزجاةٌ ، والحاضرُ مِنَ النقدِ زيفٌ ، والناقد بصيرٌ ، ولكنَّ الجودَ غزيز ، والربّ قديرٌ ، وفضل الله بالشمول جديرٌ ، فإن أقال عثراتِنا بدعاء مسلم واحد .. فما ذلك على الله بعسير مقترح في فاتحة العلوم وهأنا مقترح عليك أن تقول في دعائِكَ : اللهم ؛ أره الحق حقاً وارزقه اتباعَهُ ، وأره الباطل باطلاً وارزقه اجتنابه ، وما أحوج إلى هذا الدعاءِ مَنْ ركب متن الخطر في الارتفاع عن حضيض التقليدِ معَ سلامةِ مغبَّتِهِ ، إلى يفاع الاطلاع والاستبصار مع خطر عاقبتِهِ وتفاقم غائلتِهِ ، فإن لمْ يُرِهِ اللهُ الحق حقاً .. كَانَ نظرُهُ كلُّه هباءً ، وإن لم يوفقه للعمل بما علمه .. كان جهدُهُ كلُّهُ عناء ، وكانَ علمُهُ لجهلِهِ كفاءً ، وطاعتُهُ معَ العصيانِ سواء ، فنعوذُ باللهِ مِنْ سقطةٍ ما منها نعشة ، ومِنْ مهواة ما لها مرقاة . واعلم : أنَّ ما سمَتْ إليه همَّتُكَ ، وطَمَحَتْ نحوه عينُكَ ؛ نيل الحق مطلب النفوس الكبار إن استوفيتُهُ لكَ .. فبحر عميق ، وعمقه بعيد ، فاقنع في الحال اما بما تيسَّرَ ، وارض في الوقتِ بما حضر ، وإِنْ كانَ غَرْفَةً مِنْ بحارٍ ، وصبابة من تيَّارٍ ، وخُذها عجالة من مستوفز ، ولُمْعَةً من بارق ، وقبساً من مجتاز ، ونُبْذَةً مِنْ طارق ، واكتف بما
What is abandoned? It is said: turning to what is abandoned is burdensome, but the preciousness of the price guarantees the preciousness of the goods. And there is no price except what I hope for from the blessing of your supplication with the sincerity of your hope; that in your retreats, after your prayers; for I did not seek in what I proposed your desire, except seeking nearness to the pleasure of Allah Almighty through your pleasure. And I adjure you by the brotherhood of religion between us: do not forget me in supplication, and include with you those you know of righteous friends. For no reliance remains except the supplications of the righteous. Indeed, the truthful and believed (peace be upon him) informed: 'Supplication for the believer is a provision and a weapon.' If supplication is not the reliance, then what do we rely on? So the matter is grave, the affair serious, the journey long, the provision little, the matter dire, the life short, in action deficiency, the merchandise meager, the present currency counterfeit, the critic perceptive, but generosity abundant, the Lord powerful, and Allah's favor is worthy of inclusion. So if He forgives our stumbles through the supplication of a single Muslim, that is not difficult for Allah. A proposal in the opening of the sciences. And here I propose to you that you say in your supplication: 'O Allah, show him the truth as truth and grant him its following, and show him falsehood as falsehood and grant him its avoidance.' And how much more in need of this supplication is one who mounts the risk of rising from the lowland of imitation, with safety of outcome, to the summit of insight and discernment, with its dangerous consequence and overwhelming calamity. For if Allah does not show him the truth as truth, his entire vision is vain; and if He does not grant him success to act upon what he knows, his entire effort is toil, and his knowledge is like his ignorance, and his obedience is like disobedience. So we seek refuge in Allah from a fall from which there is no recovery, and from an abyss with no ascent. And know: that what your aspiration soars to and your eye strains towards, attaining truth is the quest of great souls. If I were to fulfill it for you, it is a deep sea, its depth distant. So be content now with what is available, and be satisfied in time with what is present, even if it be a handful from seas, a trickle from a torrent, take it hastily from a hurried one, a flash from a lightning, a spark from a passerby, a scrap from a visitor, and be content with it.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface and supplication, functioning as front matter to the logic text. It is not a logical argument but a spiritual and intellectual orientation. The author begins by acknowledging that turning to abandoned matters is heavy, but the value of the price (i.e., the reader's supplication) is proportional to the value of the goods (i.e., the benefit of the book). He humbly asks the reader to include him in prayers, especially in solitude and after obligatory prayers, emphasizing that he only sought the reader's pleasure as a means to approach Allah's pleasure. He adjures the reader by the brotherhood of faith not to forget him and to include other righteous friends. He cites the hadith that supplication is the believer's weapon, arguing that without it there is no reliance. Then he proposes a specific supplication: to ask Allah to show the truth as truth and grant its following, and falsehood as falsehood and grant its avoidance. He explains that one who ascends from imitation to insight is in grave danger; if Allah does not show truth, his vision is futile; if he is not enabled to act, his knowledge is useless and obedience equated with disobedience. He seeks refuge from irreversible falls. Finally, he advises the reader that attaining truth is the goal of great souls, but if the author were to fully deliver it, it would be a deep sea; thus the reader should be content with whatever is available, even if it is a handful from the ocean. This preface sets the tone: the text is for those who seek truth beyond blind imitation, relying on divine guidance and the prayers of the righteous.

Key Terms
الحق
الباطل

Key Takeaway
The dua is the epistemological foundation: only Allah can reveal truth and falsehood clearly; human effort must be coupled with prayer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Recite and reflect on the dua from this lesson. Then write a short paragraph connecting each of its two requests to a specific pitfall in reasoning (e.g., not recognizing falsehood as falsehood).

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Rising from Taqlid: The Danger of Imitation and the Need for Divine Aid
Author presents a supplication for guidance and reflects on the difficulty of attaining truth without divine help

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the author's view on taqlid and explain why he considers the seeker of truth to be at great risk without divine guidance.

Core Idea
Climbing from blind imitation to true insight is dangerous without divine guidance.

The author warns that the person who mounts 'the risk of rising from the lowland of imitation' is in dire need of the supplication. This lesson explores his striking imagery and its implications for the student of logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the metaphor of lowland and summit
The author describes blind imitation (taqlid) as a lowland (حضيض) and true insight (الاطلاع والاستبصار) as a summit (يفاع). The one who climbs is 'riding the risk' (ركب متن الخطر). This shows that leaving taqlid is dangerous, not comfortable.
The metaphor emphasizes that seeking truth requires courage and risk.
2
Identify the two fatal outcomes
The author lists two consequences: if Allah does not show the truth as truth, one's vision becomes vain (هباء); if one is not enabled to act, effort becomes toil (عناء) and knowledge equals ignorance. Both outcomes make the climb futile.
The dua is the remedy for both outcomes.
3
Reflect on the refuge prayer
After warning, the author seeks refuge from a fall from which there is no recovery (سقطة ما منها نعشة) and an abyss with no ascent (مهواة ما لها مرقاة). This reinforces the gravity of the journey and the absolute need for divine protection.
This is a powerful call for humility and dependence on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما هجر ، وقيل : الالتفات إلى ما هُجِرَ ثقيل ، ولكنَّ نفاسة الثمن بنفاسةِ المُثْمَنِ كفيل ، ولا ثمنَ إِلَّا ما أرجوه من بركة دعائِكَ عند صدق رجائِكَ ؛ وذلكَ في خلواتِكَ ، عند أعقاب صلواتِكَ ؛ فإنّي لم أبتغ فيما اقترحته هواكَ ، إلَّا متقرباً إلى رضا الله تعالى برضاك ، وأنا مقسم عليك بما بيننا من أخوة الدين : ألا تنساني في الدعاء ، وأن تستشركَ فيهِ مَنْ تعرفُهُ مِنْ صلحاء الأصدقاء ، فلمْ يبق معوّل سوى دعوات أهل الصلاح ؛ فقد أنب الصادق المصدوق صلوات الله عليهِ : أَنَّ الدعاء للمؤمن عُدَّةٌ وسلاح ، فإن لم يكن الدعاءُ هوَ المعوّلَ .. فعلى ماذا نتّكلُ ؟! فالأمرُ إِذْ ، والخطب جد ، والسفر طويل ، والزاد قليل ، والشأن خطير ، والعمر قصير ، وفي العمل تقصير ، والبضاعة مزجاةٌ ، والحاضرُ مِنَ النقدِ زيفٌ ، والناقد بصيرٌ ، ولكنَّ الجودَ غزيز ، والربّ قديرٌ ، وفضل الله بالشمول جديرٌ ، فإن أقال عثراتِنا بدعاء مسلم واحد .. فما ذلك على الله بعسير مقترح في فاتحة العلوم وهأنا مقترح عليك أن تقول في دعائِكَ : اللهم ؛ أره الحق حقاً وارزقه اتباعَهُ ، وأره الباطل باطلاً وارزقه اجتنابه ، وما أحوج إلى هذا الدعاءِ مَنْ ركب متن الخطر في الارتفاع عن حضيض التقليدِ معَ سلامةِ مغبَّتِهِ ، إلى يفاع الاطلاع والاستبصار مع خطر عاقبتِهِ وتفاقم غائلتِهِ ، فإن لمْ يُرِهِ اللهُ الحق حقاً .. كَانَ نظرُهُ كلُّه هباءً ، وإن لم يوفقه للعمل بما علمه .. كان جهدُهُ كلُّهُ عناء ، وكانَ علمُهُ لجهلِهِ كفاءً ، وطاعتُهُ معَ العصيانِ سواء ، فنعوذُ باللهِ مِنْ سقطةٍ ما منها نعشة ، ومِنْ مهواة ما لها مرقاة . واعلم : أنَّ ما سمَتْ إليه همَّتُكَ ، وطَمَحَتْ نحوه عينُكَ ؛ نيل الحق مطلب النفوس الكبار إن استوفيتُهُ لكَ .. فبحر عميق ، وعمقه بعيد ، فاقنع في الحال اما بما تيسَّرَ ، وارض في الوقتِ بما حضر ، وإِنْ كانَ غَرْفَةً مِنْ بحارٍ ، وصبابة من تيَّارٍ ، وخُذها عجالة من مستوفز ، ولُمْعَةً من بارق ، وقبساً من مجتاز ، ونُبْذَةً مِنْ طارق ، واكتف بما
What is abandoned? It is said: turning to what is abandoned is burdensome, but the preciousness of the price guarantees the preciousness of the goods. And there is no price except what I hope for from the blessing of your supplication with the sincerity of your hope; that in your retreats, after your prayers; for I did not seek in what I proposed your desire, except seeking nearness to the pleasure of Allah Almighty through your pleasure. And I adjure you by the brotherhood of religion between us: do not forget me in supplication, and include with you those you know of righteous friends. For no reliance remains except the supplications of the righteous. Indeed, the truthful and believed (peace be upon him) informed: 'Supplication for the believer is a provision and a weapon.' If supplication is not the reliance, then what do we rely on? So the matter is grave, the affair serious, the journey long, the provision little, the matter dire, the life short, in action deficiency, the merchandise meager, the present currency counterfeit, the critic perceptive, but generosity abundant, the Lord powerful, and Allah's favor is worthy of inclusion. So if He forgives our stumbles through the supplication of a single Muslim, that is not difficult for Allah. A proposal in the opening of the sciences. And here I propose to you that you say in your supplication: 'O Allah, show him the truth as truth and grant him its following, and show him falsehood as falsehood and grant him its avoidance.' And how much more in need of this supplication is one who mounts the risk of rising from the lowland of imitation, with safety of outcome, to the summit of insight and discernment, with its dangerous consequence and overwhelming calamity. For if Allah does not show him the truth as truth, his entire vision is vain; and if He does not grant him success to act upon what he knows, his entire effort is toil, and his knowledge is like his ignorance, and his obedience is like disobedience. So we seek refuge in Allah from a fall from which there is no recovery, and from an abyss with no ascent. And know: that what your aspiration soars to and your eye strains towards, attaining truth is the quest of great souls. If I were to fulfill it for you, it is a deep sea, its depth distant. So be content now with what is available, and be satisfied in time with what is present, even if it be a handful from seas, a trickle from a torrent, take it hastily from a hurried one, a flash from a lightning, a spark from a passerby, a scrap from a visitor, and be content with it.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface and supplication, functioning as front matter to the logic text. It is not a logical argument but a spiritual and intellectual orientation. The author begins by acknowledging that turning to abandoned matters is heavy, but the value of the price (i.e., the reader's supplication) is proportional to the value of the goods (i.e., the benefit of the book). He humbly asks the reader to include him in prayers, especially in solitude and after obligatory prayers, emphasizing that he only sought the reader's pleasure as a means to approach Allah's pleasure. He adjures the reader by the brotherhood of faith not to forget him and to include other righteous friends. He cites the hadith that supplication is the believer's weapon, arguing that without it there is no reliance. Then he proposes a specific supplication: to ask Allah to show the truth as truth and grant its following, and falsehood as falsehood and grant its avoidance. He explains that one who ascends from imitation to insight is in grave danger; if Allah does not show truth, his vision is futile; if he is not enabled to act, his knowledge is useless and obedience equated with disobedience. He seeks refuge from irreversible falls. Finally, he advises the reader that attaining truth is the goal of great souls, but if the author were to fully deliver it, it would be a deep sea; thus the reader should be content with whatever is available, even if it is a handful from the ocean. This preface sets the tone: the text is for those who seek truth beyond blind imitation, relying on divine guidance and the prayers of the righteous.

Key Terms
التقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what happens if Allah does not show the seeker the truth as truth?
A) He will become a great scholar despite it
B) His entire vision becomes vain
C) He will automatically follow the truth
D) His efforts are still rewarded

Key Takeaway
Seeking truth beyond taqlid is a perilous journey; success depends on Allah's guidance and the supplication of the righteous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a modern scenario where a person might be guilty of taqlid (blind imitation) in religious matters, and write a short analysis of the risks using the author's framework of vision and action.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
The Seekers' Humility: Why Divine Guidance is Prerequisite for Knowledge
Author opens with a prayer for guidance and introduces the condensed nature of the work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's view on the necessity of divine guidance in distinguishing truth from falsehood.

Core Idea
Divine guidance transforms knowledge from vanity to virtue.

The author opens his work with a supplication that reveals a foundational principle: without God's help, even the most rigorous inquiry can lead to error. This lesson unpacks that supplication and its implications for the seeker of truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two-part supplication
Find the two requests in the opening: to see truth as truth and falsehood as falsehood, and to follow truth and avoid falsehood.
2
Recognize the danger of rising from taqlid
The author warns that ascending from blind imitation to insight is perilous without divine guidance.
3
Understand the consequence of missing divine aid
If God does not show the truth, all effort becomes dust; if He does not grant action, knowledge becomes equivalent to ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حقاً وارزقه اتباعَهُ ، وأره الباطل باطلاً وارزقه اجتنابه ، وما أحوج إلى هذا الدعاءِ مَنْ ركب متن الخطر في الارتفاع عن حضيض التقليدِ معَ سلامةِ مغبَّتِهِ ، إلى يفاع الاطلاع والاستبصار مع خطر عاقبتِهِ وتفاقم غائلتِهِ ، فإن لمْ يُرِهِ اللهُ الحق حقاً .. كَانَ نظرُهُ كلُّه هباءً ، وإن لم يوفقه للعمل بما علمه .. كان جهدُهُ كلُّهُ عناء ، وكانَ علمُهُ لجهلِهِ كفاءً ، وطاعتُهُ معَ العصيانِ سواء ، فنعوذُ باللهِ مِنْ سقطةٍ ما منها نعشة ، ومِنْ مهواة ما لها مرقاة . واعلم : أنَّ ما سمَتْ إليه همَّتُكَ ، وطَمَحَتْ نحوه عينُكَ ؛ نيل الحق مطلب النفوس الكبار إن استوفيتُهُ لكَ .. فبحر عميق ، وعمقه بعيد ، فاقنع في الحال اما بما تيسَّرَ ، وارض في الوقتِ بما حضر ، وإِنْ كانَ غَرْفَةً مِنْ بحارٍ ، وصبابة من تيَّارٍ ، وخُذها عجالة من مستوفز ، ولُمْعَةً من بارق ، وقبساً من مجتاز ، ونُبْذَةً مِنْ طارق ، واكتفِ بما سمحت به القريحة على ارتجالها ، وأذعنَتْ للتشاغل به النفس مع ارتحالها . وتحقَّق أنِّي جامع لكَ معَ الإيجاز مِنَ النُّكَتِ النفيسة زبدة مخضها ، وصفوة محضها ، ومشير إلى جمل إذا أخذ التوفيق بضَبْعِكَ ، ومال إلى استدرارِ فوائدِهِ واستخراج ودائعه وبدائعه فهمك وطبعكَ .. احتویتَ بهِ على ما انتحيتَ ، واستوليت على ما ابتغيت . والله تعالى يعصم أقوالنا عمَّا يراه بكمال علمِهِ خطاً وخُلْفاً ، ويوفقنا لما يقربنا إليهِ زُلْفَىٰ ، بمنّه وفضلِهِ
Show him the truth as truth and grant him its following, and show him falsehood as falsehood and grant him its avoidance. How much more in need of this supplication is the one who rides the crest of danger in rising from the lowland of imitation with a safe outcome, to the height of insight and discernment with the danger of its consequence and the enormity of its calamity. For if God does not show him the truth as truth, all his vision becomes dust; and if He does not grant him success to act upon what he knows, all his effort becomes fatigue, and his knowledge becomes equivalent to his ignorance, and his obedience equals disobedience. So we seek refuge with God from a fall from which there is no recovery, and from an abyss from which there is no ascent. And know: what you aspire to with your ambition and your eye gazes toward—attaining truth is the quest of great souls. If I were to fulfill it fully for you, it is a deep sea, its depth far. So be content for now with what is made easy, and be satisfied at the time with what is present, even if it is a scoop from the seas, a sip from a torrent. Take it as a quick offering from a one hastening, a flash from lightning, an ember from a passerby, a piece from a night-traveler. And be content with what the mind has allowed in its improvisation and the soul has submitted to occupying itself with despite its departure. And realize that I am gathering for you, with conciseness, from precious subtleties: the cream after churning, the purest after refinement, and indicating summaries. If success takes your arm, and your understanding and nature incline toward drawing out its benefits and extracting its treasures and novelties, you will encompass what you aimed for and take possession of what you sought. And God Almighty protects our statements from what He, with His perfect knowledge, sees as error and contradiction, and grants us success to that which brings us near to Him, through His grace and bounty.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface or introductory remarks in which he sets the tone for the book. He begins with a well-known prayer asking God to show truth as truth and falsehood as falsehood, and to grant the ability to follow truth and avoid falsehood. He then emphasizes the peril of seeking knowledge without divine guidance: one who rises from blind imitation to insight risks great danger, and without God's help, his efforts are futile. The author then addresses the reader directly, acknowledging that attaining truth is the goal of great souls, but that a complete exposition is beyond the scope of this work; it is like a deep sea. He counsels the reader to be content with what is presented here, even if it is only a small portion, using metaphors such as a scoop from the sea or a flash of lightning. He describes the book as a condensed collection of precious subtleties, the cream of knowledge, and expresses hope that if the reader is divinely aided, they will benefit greatly. The passage concludes with a concluding supplication for divine protection from error and for success in drawing near to God.

Key Terms
حضيض التقليد (hadīḍ al-taqlīd): The lowland of blind imitation; refers to the state of following without understanding or evidence. [Note: This term is used here in a negative sense, contrasting with independent inquiry.]
يفاع الاطلاع والاستبصار (yafā' al-iṭṭilā' wa-al-istibṣār): The height of insight and discernment; a metaphorical expression for attaining true knowledge.
النفوس الكبار (al-nufūs al-kibār): Great souls; refers to those with lofty aspirations who seek truth.
زبدة مخضها (zubda makhḍihā): The cream after churning; metaphor for the most valuable essence extracted from knowledge.
صفوة محضها (ṣafwa maḥḍihā): The purest after refinement; another metaphor for the distilled essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All his vision becomes dust
B) He falls into complete ignorance
C) He remains in taqlid
D) He must start over

Key Takeaway
Divine guidance is indispensable for attaining certain knowledge; without it, even the most earnest search may end in vanity.

?

Condensation as a Pedagogical Virtue: The Author's Metaphors
Author opens with a prayer for guidance and introduces the condensed nature of the work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret the author's metaphors for his concise work and assess their pedagogical purpose.

Core Idea
Concise knowledge is valuable if it captures the essence; like cream from milk.

The author describes his work as a scoop from the sea, a flash of lightning, an ember from a passerby. These metaphors are not self-deprecation but a deliberate pedagogical strategy. This lesson examines how conciseness (ijaz) is a respected scholarly method.

Guided Path
1
List the metaphors used
Find the series of metaphors: scoop from the sea, sip from a torrent, quick offering, flash of lightning, ember, piece from a night-traveler.
2
Identify the common theme
Each metaphor represents a small part of something larger, yet each is valuable and sufficient for immediate benefit.
3
Connect to the author's intention
The author states he is gathering the 'cream after churning' and 'purest after refinement', indicating his work contains the essence despite its brevity.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Two Kinds of Perception: Conception vs. Assent
Author divides perception into simple and relational, introduces alternative terminology from grammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simple conception (tasawwur) and judgment (tasdiq) and explain the author's alternative terminology using grammatical transitivity.

Core Idea
Knowing a concept and knowing a truth are two fundamentally different acts of perception.

The author opens the book by reminding us that logic aims to define and syllogize correctly. But before that, we need a clear picture of what knowledge itself is—and that splits into two kinds: grasping a simple concept, and judging a relation between concepts. These are the building blocks of all reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two categories
The author gives examples: 'body', 'motion', 'world' are simple concepts (tasawwur); 'the world is temporal' is a judgment (tasdiq). Notice that simple concepts are the ingredients, while judgments are the dish made from them.
2
Spot the grammatical link
The author borrows from Arabic grammar: verbs taking one object (عَرَفَ, 'I knew Zayd') correspond to simple conception; verbs taking two objects (عَلِمَ, 'I knew Zayd to be learned') correspond to judgment. This shows that judgments inherently involve a relation between two things.
3
Understand truth and falsehood
Only judgments can be true or false, because truth is about whether the asserted relation actually holds. Simple concepts, taken alone, are neither true nor false. This is why logic focuses on propositions (qadaya) when assessing validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أنك التمست شرط القياس الصحيح والحدّ الصحيح ، والتنبية على مثارات الغلط فيهما ، ونعم ما وفقت للجمع بين الأمرين ؛ فإنهما رباط العلوم كلها ؛ فإن إدراك الأمور على ضربين : إدراك الذوات المفردة : كعلمك بمعنى الجسم والحركة والعالم والحادث والقديم وسائر المفردات . وإدراك نسبة هذه المفردات بعضها إلى بعض بالنفي أو الإثبات : فإنك تعلم أولاً معنى لفظ ( العالم ) وهو أمر مفرد ، ومعنى لفظ ( الحادث ) ومعنى لفظ ( القديم ) وهما أيضاً أمران مفردان ، ثم تنسب مفرداً إلى مفرد بالنفي أو الإثبات ، كما تنسب ( القديم ) إلى ( العالم ) بالنفي ، فتقول : ( ليس العالم قديماً ) ، وتنسب ( الحادث ) إليه بالإثبات ، فتقول : ( العالم حادث ) . والضرب الأخير هو الذي يتطرق إليه التصديق والتكذيب ، وأما التصديق فلا يتطرق للمفردات ، بل الأول فلا يدخله تصديق ولا تكذيب ؛ إذ يستحيل التصديق والتكذيب في المفردات ، بل إنما يتطرق ذلك إلى الخبر ، ولا ينتظم خبر إلا بمفردين : وصف وموصوف ، فإذا نسب الوصف إلى الموصوف بنفي أو إثبات .. صدق أو كذب . ولا بأس أن نصطلح على التعبير عن هذين الضربين بعبارتين : فلنسم الأول معرفة ، ولنسم الثاني علماً ، متأسين فيه بقول النحاة : إن المعرفة تتعدى إلى مفعول واحد ؛ إذ تقول : عرفت زيداً ، والظن يتعدى إلى مفعولين ؛ إذ تقول : ظننت زيداً عالماً ، ولا تقول : ظننت زيداً ، ولا : ظننت عالماً ، وكذا تقول : خلت زيداً عالماً عدلاً . والعلم أيضاً يتعدى إلى مفعولين ؛ إذ تقول : علمت زيداً ، فهو من باب الظن ، لا من باب المعرفة ، وهذا هو الوضع اللغوي ، وإن كانت عبارة أهل النظر ربما تخالفه في استعمال أحدهما بدلاً عن الآخر . فإذا استقر هذا الاصطلاح .. فنقول : الإدراكات المعلومة
Know: You have sought the condition of valid syllogism and valid definition, and the alerting to the sources of error in them, and how well you have succeeded in combining the two matters; for they are the bond of all sciences. For the perception of things is of two types: perception of simple entities, such as your knowledge of the meaning of body, motion, world, temporal, eternal, and the rest of the simples. And perception of the relation of these simples to each other by negation or affirmation: for you first know the meaning of the word 'world' — it is a simple matter, and the meaning of the word 'temporal' and the meaning of the word 'eternal' — they also are two simple matters, then you relate a simple to a simple by negation or affirmation, as you relate 'eternal' to 'world' by negation, saying: 'The world is not eternal', and you relate 'temporal' to it by affirmation, saying: 'The world is temporal'. And the second type is what is susceptible to affirmation and falsification; but the first is not entered by affirmation nor falsification; for it is impossible to have affirmation and falsification in simples, rather that pertains to the statement, and a statement is not composed except of two simples: a description and a described; so when the description is related to the described by negation or affirmation, it becomes true or false. And it is no harm that we adopt a convention for expressing these two types with two phrases: let us name the first 'ma'rifa' and name the second 'ilm', following the grammarians' statement: 'ma'rifa' is transitive to one object; for you say: 'I knew Zayd', and 'zann' is transitive to two objects; for you say: 'I thought Zayd is learned', and you do not say: 'I thought Zayd', nor: 'I thought learned'; similarly you say: 'I deemed Zayd to be learned and just'. And 'ilm' also is transitive to two objects; for you say: 'I knew Zayd to be learned', so it is of the category of 'zann', not of the category of 'ma'rifa'; and this is the linguistic usage, even if the expression of the scholars of speculation perhaps contradicts it in using one in place of the other. So when this convention is settled... we say: the known perceptions...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage opens the book by stating its overarching purpose: to clarify the conditions of a valid syllogism and a valid definition (hadd), and to warn against the points where errors creep in. The author then introduces a foundational epistemological division: all perception (idrak) is of two kinds. First, perception of simple entities (dhawat mufrada) — for example, knowing the meaning of 'body', 'motion', 'world', 'temporal', 'eternal'. Second, perception of a relation (nisba) between these simples, which can be affirmative or negative — for example, after knowing the simple concepts of 'world' and 'temporal', one judges 'The world is temporal'. This second kind is what admits truth or falsehood (tasdiq and takdhib), because it is a statement (khabar) composed of two simples: a subject (mawsoof) and a predicate (sifa). The first kind, simple conception, does not admit truth or falsehood by itself. The author then proposes a terminological convention: to call the first kind 'ma'rifa' (knowledge by acquaintance, taking one grammatical object) and the second kind 'ilm' (knowledge by judgment, taking two grammatical objects), in imitation of the grammarians' distinction between verbs like 'arafa (with one object) and verbs like zanna and alima (with two objects). He acknowledges that the scholars of speculative theology might sometimes use the terms interchangeably, but for the purposes of this work he insists on this distinction.

تصور
تصديق
معرفة
علم

Key Takeaway
The foundational division of knowledge into simple conception (tasawwur/ma'rifa) and judgment (tasdiq/'ilm) underlies all logical reasoning about definitions and syllogisms.

Understanding the Division of Knowledge: Concept and Assent
Author divides knowledge into concept and assent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent) and explain why concept must precede assent using the author's own example.

Core Idea
Every judgment requires prior understanding of its simple terms.

The author opens by laying the epistemological foundation of logic: all knowledge is either a simple concept or a judgment. He argues that you cannot judge a compound statement unless you know the meanings of its parts. This is a crucial insight that determines the structure of logical inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of knowledge
The author says all knowledge (علم) is confined to two: tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). Find the Arabic terms and note their definitions: tasawwur is simple apprehension; tasdiq is judgment.
These categories are exhaustive.
2
Understand the dependency relation
The author asserts that every assent necessarily requires two prior concepts. For example, to know that 'the world is originated,' you first need the concepts of 'world' and 'originated.' He asks rhetorically: how can one know a compound without knowing the simples?
This shows the priority of concept.
3
Analyse the author's example
Analyse the given example: 'The world is originated' is a judgment (tasdiq). The concepts needed are 'world' and 'originated.' Without these, the judgment cannot be formed.
4
Apply the principle to another Islamic example
Provide your own example: to know that 'Zakat is obligatory,' you need concepts of 'Zakat' and 'obligatory.' This illustrates the universal dependency of assent on concepts.
This principle underlies all logical and theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنحصر في المعرفة والعلم ، وكلُّ علم يتطرق إليه التصديقُ فمِنْ ضرورتِهِ أن يتقدَّمَ عليهِ معرفتانِ ؛ فإِنَّ مَن لا يعرفُ المفرد .. كيف يعلمُ المركَّبَ ؟! ومَن لا يفهم معنى ( العالم ) ومعنى ( الحادثِ ) . . كيف يعلم أن العالم حادث ؟! والمعرفة قسمان : أولي : وهوَ الذي لا يُطلبُ بالبحث ؛ كالمفردات المدركة بالحس . ومطلوب : وهو الذي يدلَّ اسمُهُ منه على أمرٍ جُمْلي غير مفصَّلٍ فيُطلب تفصيلُهُ وكذلك العلم ينقسم إلى أولي ، وإلى مطلوب . والمطلوبُ مِنَ المعرفةِ لا يُقتنصُ إلَّا بالحدّ ، والمطلوبُ مِنَ العلم الذي يتطرَّقُ إليه التصديق أو التكذيب لا يُقتنصُ إِلَّا بالحجَّةِ والبرهان ، وهو القياسُ ، وكأنَّ طالب القياس والحدّ طالب الآلة التي بها تقتنص العلوم والمعارف كلها • فليكن كتابنا قسمين : قسم هوَ مِحَثُ القياس ، وقسم هوَ مِحَلٌّ الحد .
It is confined to concept and knowledge. Every knowledge to which assent pertains necessarily requires two preceding concepts: for whoever does not know the simple, how can he know the compound? And whoever does not understand the meaning of 'the world' and the meaning of 'the originated', how can he know that the world is originated? And concept is of two types: primary — that which is not sought by investigation, like simple things perceived by the senses; and acquired — that whose name indicates a summary, non-detailed thing, so its detail is sought. Likewise, knowledge is divided into primary and acquired. The acquired from concept is not obtained except by definition, and the acquired from knowledge to which assent or denial pertains is not obtained except by proof and demonstration, which is the syllogism. And it is as if the seeker of syllogism and definition seeks the tool by which all sciences and concepts are obtained. So let our book be two parts: a part that is the touchstone of syllogism, and a part that is the touchstone of definition.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction to the science of logic. The author establishes a fundamental epistemological framework: all knowledge (علم) is divided into two categories — tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). Assent requires prior concepts; you cannot judge a compound proposition without knowing the simple terms. The author uses the example: to know that the world is originated (حادث), you must first understand the concepts of 'world' and 'originated'.Next, the author divides both concept and knowledge into two types: primary (أولي) and acquired (مطلوب). Primary concepts are self-evident, like sense perceptions; acquired concepts are those whose meaning is known in a summary way and require detailing through definition (حد). Similarly, primary knowledge is self-evident, and acquired knowledge requires proof (حجة) or demonstration (برهان), which is the syllogism (قياس).The key claim: the method for obtaining acquired concepts is definition (حد), and the method for obtaining acquired assent is demonstration (برهان). This is a foundational principle in Islamic logic. The author then announces the structure of the book: it will have two parts, one on the syllogism (القياس) and one on definition (الحد), each acting as a 'touchstone' (محك) for its subject. The phrase 'touchstone' (محك) in the title Mahakk al-Nazar implies that logic is the instrument for testing and evaluating arguments and definitions.

تصديق
أولي
مطلوب

Key Takeaway
All knowledge is either a simple concept (tasawwur) or a judgment (tasdiq), and every judgment depends on prior concepts.

Primary and Acquired Knowledge: Tools of Definition and Demonstration
Author divides knowledge into primary and acquired, assigns tools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between primary and acquired knowledge, and specify which tool (hadd or burhan) corresponds to which type.

Core Idea
Primary knowledge is self-evident; acquired knowledge requires definition for concepts and demonstration for judgments.

After establishing the two categories, the author further divides each into primary and acquired. This is crucial because it tells us what needs investigation and what does not. The tools of definition (hadd) and demonstration (burhan) are the means to obtain acquired knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish primary from acquired in concepts
The author says concept (ma'rifa) is either awwali (primary) – not sought by investigation, like sensory perceptions – or matlub (acquired) – known summarily but needing detail. Find these in the Arabic.
Primary concepts are self-evident.
2
Distinguish primary from acquired in knowledge (assent)
Similarly, knowledge (علم) is divided into primary and acquired. Primary knowledge is self-evident (e.g., 'the whole is greater than the part'); acquired knowledge needs proof.
This parallels the division in concepts.
3
Identify the method for acquired concepts
The acquired concept is obtained only by definition (hadd). So to clarify a term like 'substance', you need a definition that provides its genus and differentia.
Hadd is the tool for concepts.
4
Identify the method for acquired assent
The acquired knowledge that involves assent or denial is obtained only by proof (hujja) or demonstration (burhan), which is the syllogism (qiyas). So any claim that is not self-evident must be demonstrated.
Burhan is the tool for judgments.
5
Connect to the book's structure
The author states that his book will be two parts: the touchstone of syllogism and the touchstone of definition. This structure directly reflects the two tools.
'Touchstone' (محك) means testing instrument.

Introduction to the First Section: On Syllogism
Section heading on syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define syllogism (qiyas) within the Islamic logical tradition and identify its role as the foundation of formal inference.

Core Idea
Syllogism is the engine of certain inference in Islamic logic.

This lesson introduces the first major section of Mahakk al-Nazar, which is devoted entirely to syllogism (qiyas). Before diving into the technical details of figures and moods, we need to understand what a syllogism is and why it is so central to the logical tradition. This short heading marks the beginning of that exploration.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the term 'qiyas'
Note that 'qiyas' here means logical syllogism, not analogical reasoning in fiqh. In logic, it is a structured argument where premises entail a conclusion.
The shift from fiqh to logical terminology is important to avoid confusion.
2
Understand the section's purpose
This section will systematically present the conditions, figures, and moods of valid syllogisms. It is the core of Aristotelian logic as adopted by Islamic scholars.
3
Prepare for upcoming details
Anticipate that the following lessons will define syllogism formally, distinguish categorical from conditional types, and explain how to test validity.
This heading serves as a signpost for the journey ahead.

The Foundations of Logic: Composites Depend on Simple Components
Author establishes the composite-defect principle and building analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logical study must begin with simple components, using the author's composite-defect principle and the building analogy.

Core Idea
Every defect in a composite is either in its form or its matter; one must master the simples first.

The author opens with a powerful observation: any composite thing, whether a shirt, a chair, or a wall, can have a defect either in its structure (the way it is put together) or in the quality of its parts. This principle, he argues, applies directly to logic. If we want to build sound arguments, we must first understand the simple building blocks. In this lesson, we will explore why the study of logic cannot skip straight to syllogisms without first mastering the simple concepts and terms that compose them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the composite-defect principle
The author states that any composite (murakkab) can have a defect either in its form (hay'a) or in its matter (asl). Find the examples he gives (shirt, chair, wall, ceiling) and note how each defect falls into one of these categories.
This principle is foundational for understanding why logical analysis must consider both structure and content.
2
Analyze the building analogy
The author compares studying logic to building a house. A builder prepares simple materials (beams, bricks, clay) before assembling them. He must even prepare the components of those simples (water, straw, soil for bricks). What does this analogy teach about the order of learning?
The analogy stresses that preparation of simples is not optional but essential for a sound result.
3
Apply the principle to logic
The author explicitly says the syllogism (qiyas) and definition (hadd) are composites. Therefore, their study must begin with their simplest parts. What are those simplest parts according to the author?
He will later define them as premises (muqaddimat), then concepts (ma'rifat), then terms (alfaz) and meanings (ma'ani).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسقفِهِ ، ولكن يكون الاختلالُ مِنْ رَخاوة في الجذوع ، وتشعُثِ في اللبنات (١) فهذا حكمُ القياس والحدّ وكلّ أمرٍ مركَّب ؛ فإِنَّ الخلل فيهِ إِمَّا أن يكونَ في هيئة تركيبه وترتيبه ، وإما أن يكون في الأصل الذي يرد عليه التركيب ؛ كالثوبِ في القميص ، والخشب في الكرسي ، واللَّبِنِ في الحائط ، والجذوع في السقف. وكما أَنَّ مَنْ يريدُ بناءَ بيتٍ بعيد عن الخلل يفتقر إلى أن يعِدَّ الآلات المفردة أولاً ؛ كالجذوع واللَّبِنِ والطين ، ثمَّ إذا أرادَ اللَّبِنَ افتقر إلى إعدادِ مفرداتِهِ ؛ وهي الماءُ والتبن والتراب ، والقالب الذي فيه يضربُ ، فيبتدئ أولاً بالأجزاء المفردة فيركبها، ثمَّ يركّب المركَّبَ ، وهكذا إلى آخرِ العمل .. فكذلك طالب القياس ينبغي أن ينظر في نظم القياس وفي صورتِهِ ، وفي الأمر الذي يضعُ الترتيب والنظم فيهِ ، وهو المقدمات . وأقلُّ ما ينتظمُ منهُ قياس مقدمتان ـ أعني : علمين - يتطرَّقُ إليهما التصديق والتكذيب. وأقل ما تحصلُ منه مقدمةٌ، معرفتانِ ، توضعُ إحداهما مُخبراً عنه ، والأخرى خبراً أو وصفاً. فقد انقسم القياسُ إلى مقدمتين ، وانقسمَتْ كلُّ مقدمة إلى معرفتين تنسب إحداهما إلى الأخرى . وكلُّ مفردٍ فهوَ معنى ، ويُدَلُّ عليه - لا محالة - بلفظ. فيجب ضرورةً أن ننظر في المعاني المفردة وأقسامها ، وفي الألفاظ المفردة ووجوه دلالتِها ، ثمَّ إذا فهمنا اللفظ مفرداً والمعنى الصحيح مفرداً .. ألفنا معنيين ، وجعلناهما مقدمةً ، وننظر في حكم المقدمة وشرطها ، ثمَّ نجمعَ مقدمتين ، فنصوغ منهما قياساً ، وننظر في كيفية الصياغة الصحيحة . وكلُّ مَنْ أراد أن يعرف القياس بغير هذا الطريق .. فقد طمع في محالٍ ، وكانَ كَمَنْ طمعَ في أن يكون كاتباً يكتب الخطوط المنظومة وهوَ لا يحسنُ كَتْبةَ الكلمات ، أو يطمع أن يكتب الكلماتِ وهوَ لا يحسنُ كَتْبةَ الحروفِ المفردةِ . وهكذا القول في كل مركَّب ؛ فإِنَّ أجزاء المركَبِ تتقدَّمُ على المركّب بالضرورة ، حتَّى لا يُوصفُ اللهُ تعالى بالقدرة على خلق العالم المركَبِ دونَ الآحادِ ، كما لا يُوصف بالقدرة على تعليمِ كتبة الخطوط المنظومة دونَ تعليم الكلمات والحروف. فبهذه الضرورةِ ينبغي أن يشتمل كلامنا في القياس على ثلاثة فنون :
And its ceiling, but the defect may be due to weakness in the beams and crumbling in the bricks (1). This is the rule of the syllogism and the definition and every composite matter: for the defect in it is either in the form of its composition and arrangement, or in the material upon which composition occurs; like the cloth in the shirt, the wood in the chair, the brick in the wall, and the beams in the ceiling. And just as one who wants to build a house free from defect needs to prepare the simple tools first, like the beams, bricks, and clay; then if he wants the brick, he needs to prepare its simple components: water, straw, soil, and the mold in which it is struck; so he begins first with the simple parts, combines them, then combines the composite, and so on to the end of the work. Similarly, the student of the syllogism should consider the order of the syllogism and its form, and the thing in which arrangement and order are placed, namely the premises. The least from which a syllogism is composed is two premises – that is, two items of knowledge – which are susceptible to affirmation and denial. The least from which a premise is obtained is two concepts, one of them placed as the subject of predication and the other as predicate or description. Thus the syllogism is divided into two premises, and each premise is divided into two concepts, one of which is related to the other. Every simple thing is a meaning, and it is necessarily indicated by a term. Therefore, it is necessary that we consider the simple meanings and their divisions, and the simple terms and their modes of indication. Then, when we understand the term as simple and the correct meaning as simple, we combine two meanings, make them a premise, and consider the rule and condition of the premise; then we combine two premises, formulate from them a syllogism, and consider the correct method of formulation. Whoever wants to know the syllogism by any other path has desired something impossible, and is like one who desires to be a writer writing joined letters while not being skilled in writing words, or desires to write words while not being skilled in writing individual letters. The same applies to every composite: for the parts of the composite necessarily precede the composite, to the extent that Allah, exalted, is not described as having power to create the composite world without the individuals, just as He is not described as having power to teach the writing of joined letters without teaching words and letters. By this necessity, our discussion of the syllogism should include three arts:
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction that establishes the foundational principle for studying logic: one must begin with the simplest components before moving to composites. The author, likely Ibn Sina or a follower, argues that any composite (murakkab) can have defects in either its form (structure) or its matter (components). He illustrates this with concrete examples: a shirt's defect may be in the cloth or the stitching; a chair's defect in the wood or the joints; a wall's defect in the bricks or the mortar; a ceiling's defect in the beams or the plaster. This principle applies to syllogism (qiyas) and definition (hadd) as well. The author then draws an analogy to building a house: a builder first prepares simple tools (beams, bricks, clay), then if he needs bricks, he prepares their simple components (water, straw, soil, mold). He starts with simple parts, combines them into composites, and continues until the building is complete. Similarly, a student of logic must first examine the structure and form of the syllogism, and the material in which this structure is placed—the premises. The minimal syllogism consists of two premises (muqaddimat), which are two items of knowledge (ilman) that can be true or false. Each premise minimally consists of two concepts (ma'rifatan), one serving as subject (mukhbar 'anh) and the other as predicate (khabar). Thus the syllogism divides into two premises, and each premise divides into two concepts that are related. Every simple (mufrad) is a meaning (ma'na) and is necessarily indicated by a term (lafz). Therefore, we must first study simple meanings and their categories, and simple terms and their modes of indication. Only after understanding individual terms and meanings do we combine two meanings into a premise, examine the rules and conditions of premises, then combine two premises into a syllogism and study the correct method of formulation. The author strongly warns against skipping this order: anyone who tries to understand syllogism without this step-by-step path is like someone who wants to write joined letters without knowing how to write individual words, or wants to write words without knowing individual letters. This necessity is so absolute that even Allah's power is not described as creating composite things without the simple components (atoms), nor teaching joined writing without teaching letters and words. Thus, the author declares that the discussion of syllogism must cover three arts (funun), which will structure the remainder of the book.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what are the two possible sources of defect in any composite?
A) The form of composition and the material upon which composition occurs
B) The premises and the conclusion
C) The subject and the predicate
D) The term and the meaning

Key Takeaway
Composite things, including syllogisms, can only be properly understood and evaluated if one first masters their simple components.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a legal ruling that is a composite of several premises (e.g., 'Wine is forbidden because it is intoxicating; all intoxicants are forbidden'). Using the author's principle, identify the simple components (the meaning of 'wine', 'intoxicant', 'forbidden'). Then explain why each simple must be understood before the composite ruling can be evaluated.
?
Find an example in your own study of an Islamic science (e.g., Fiqh, Kalam, Tafsir) where a composite argument depends on simple elements. Write a short paragraph showing how the author's principle applies.
